Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa
advanced
8 min
The Hierarchy of Sources for the Hanafi Mufti
Poem on the hierarchy of Hanafi sources for fatwa

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the hierarchy of Hanafi legal sources (Zahir al-Riwayah, Nawadir, Nawazil) and apply the rule that Zahir al-Riwayah takes precedence unless later scholars have preferred otherwise.

Core Idea
The mufti must follow the preponderant source: Zahir al-Riwayah, then Nawadir, then Nawazil.

This lesson unpacks a foundational maxim from the Rasm al-Mufti tradition, clarifying the order of authority among Hanafi sources. The poem by Ibn Kamal Pasha (or similar) lays down a clear hierarchy: the six books of Zahir al-Riwayah are primary, followed by rare transmissions (Nawadir), and then later legal cases (Nawazil). Understanding this sequence is essential for a mufti to issue correct fatwas.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Maxim
The first line states the obligation to follow the preponderant (tarjih) from the reliable authorities. Recognize that this is the governing principle.
2
List the Three Categories
The poem mentions three types: Zahir al-Riwayah (six books), Nawadir (rare transmissions), and Nawazil (later derived cases). Note their order.
3
Detail the Six Books
Name the six books: al-Jami' al-Saghir, al-Jami' al-Kabir, al-Siyar al-Kabir, al-Siyar al-Saghir, al-Ziyadat, and al-Mabsut. Understand their relative precedence: al-Mabsut is earliest but al-Jami' al-Saghir takes precedence because it is based on al-Asl; al-Siyar al-Kabir is last and mu'tamad.
4
Explain the Role of Later Works
Al-Kafi by al-Hakim al-Shahid collects the six, and its strongest commentary is al-Mabsut of Shams al-Aimmah al-Sarakhsi. This is the ultimate authority for transmissions.
5
Apply the Hierarchy
When giving a fatwa, first check the six books. If a ruling is found and no later scholar has preferred otherwise, it is binding. If not, move to Nawadir, then Nawazil.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعْلَمْ بِأنَ الْوَاجِبَ اتَّبَاعُ مَا تَرجِيْحُه عَنْ أهْلِهِ قَدْ عُلِمَا. أو كَانَ ظَاهِرَ الرِّوَايَةِ وَلَمْ يُرجَحُوا خِلافَ ذَاكَ فاعْلَمُ وَكُتُبُ ظَاهِرِ الرِّوَايَاتِ أَتَتْ سِنَا وَبِالأَصُولِ أَيْضًا سُمّيت صَنْفَهَا مُحَمَّدٌ الشَيْبَانِيِّ فِيهَا المَذْهَبَ النُعْمَانِي الجَامِعُ الصَّغِيرُ وَالكَبِيرُ وَالسِّيرُ الكَبِيرُ والصَّغِيرُ الزِيَادَاتُ مَعَ المَبْسُوطِ تَوَاتَرَتْ بِالسَّنَدِ المَضْبُوط ثُمّ كَذَا لَه مَسَائِلُ النَّوَادِرُ إِسْنَادُهَا فِي الْكُتُبِ غَيرُ ظَاهِرُ وَبَعْدَهَا مَسَائِلُ النَّوَازِلَ خَرَّجَهَا الأَشْيَاحُ بالدَّلائِل وَاشْتَهَرَ المَبْسُوط بالأَصْلِ وَذَا بقه السيّةَ تَصْنيفا كَذَا الجَامِعُ الصَّغِيرُ بَعْدَهُ فَمَا عَلَى الأَصْلِ لِذَا تَقَدِّما وَآخِرُ السِّنَةِ تَصْنِيفًا وَرَدْ السِّيَرَ الكَبِيْرُ فَهُوَ المُعْتَمَ ويَجْمَعُ الستَ كِتَابُ الكَافِى الأئمة لِلحَاكِمِ الشَّهِيدِ فَهُوَ الكَافِي أقوَى شُروحِه الذي كالشمس مَبسُوط شمس الأمة السرخسى مُعتَمَدُ النُّقُولِ لَيسَ يُعمَل بِخَلْفِه وَلَيْسَ عَنْهُ يُعْدَل
Know that it is obligatory to follow what is preponderant according to its people, or what is Zahir al-Riwayah, if they have not preferred its opposite. The books of Zahir al-Riwayah came as six, also called al-Usul. They were authored by Muhammad al-Shaybani, containing the Nu'mani madhhab. Al-Jami' al-Saghir, al-Kabir, al-Siyar al-Kabir, al-Saghir, al-Ziyadat with al-Mabsut; they were transmitted with established chain. Then there are his Masail al-Nawadir, whose chains in the books are not apparent. After them are Masail al-Nawazil, which the teachers extracted with evidence. Al-Mabsut is known as al-Asl, and it is earlier in composition. Al-Jami' al-Saghir follows it, but it is based on al-Asl, so it takes precedence. The last in composition is al-Siyar al-Kabir; it is the relied upon. And the book al-Kafi by al-Hakim al-Shahid collects the six; it is sufficient. Its strongest commentary is Mabsut Shams al-Aimmah al-Sarakhsi, like the sun. It is the relied upon for transmissions, no action contrary to it, and no deviation from it.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage establishes the core hierarchy of sources for the Hanafi mufti. First, the mufti must follow what is preponderant (tarjih) from the reliable early authorities, or the explicit text of Zahir al-Riwayah if no contrary preference. The six books authored by Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani are the primary authoritative sources, also known as al-Usul. They are: 1) al-Jami' al-Saghir, 2) al-Jami' al-Kabir, 3) al-Siyar al-Kabir, 4) al-Siyar al-Saghir, 5) al-Ziyadat, 6) al-Mabsut. These have verified chains. Then come Masail al-Nawadir, which are rare transmissions with weaker chains. Then Masail al-Nawazil, later cases derived by later scholars. Among the six, al-Mabsut (also called al-Asl) is considered the earliest; al-Jami' al-Saghir comes after in composition but is based on al-Asl, so it takes precedence. The last composed is al-Siyar al-Kabir, which is the relied-upon for its topics. Al-Kafi by al-Hakim al-Shahid compiles the six. The strongest commentary of al-Kafi is al-Mabsut of Shams al-Aimmah al-Sarakhsi, which is the ultimate authority for transmission; no one may act contrary to it.

Key Terms
ظَاهِر الرِّوَايَة (Zahir al-Riwayah): The six books compiled by Imam Muhammad that are well-known and widely transmitted among Hanafis
النَّوَادِر (al-Nawadir): Rare or less reliable transmissions with weak chains
النَّوَازِل (al-Nawazil): Later legal cases derived by later scholars based on evidence

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the poem, which of the six books of Zahir al-Riwayah is the last in composition and is considered the relied-upon (mu'tamad) source?
A) Al-Mabsut
B) Al-Jami' al-Saghir
C) Al-Siyar al-Kabir
D) Al-Ziyadat

Key Takeaway
The mufti must base fatwa on Zahir al-Riwayah first, then Nawadir, then Nawazil, following the preponderant of the early authorities.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa
intermediate
8 min
Hierarchy of Hanafi Imams and Later Tarjih
A poem establishing the hierarchy and methodology for preferring Hanafi opinions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the order of preference among Hanafi authorities and apply the later tarjih methodology to determine the mufta bihi opinion.

Core Idea
The mufti must follow the corrected opinions of later scholars, not free choice.

This lesson unpacks a foundational poem on the Hanafi fatwa hierarchy. You will learn the primary order of imams—Abu Hanifa, then Abu Yusuf, then Muhammad, then Zufar—and the rules for preferring opinions when they conflict, especially in later times when independent ijtihad is no longer expected.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Primary Sources
The poem begins with reports from Abu Hanifa; he selected some, and companions select from him. Establish that the foundational source is Abu Hanifa's own choices.
2
Order of Preference After Abu Hanifa
When no explicit choice from Abu Hanifa is found, the order is: Abu Yusuf (Ya'qub), then Muhammad al-Shaybani, then Zufar, then Hasan ibn Ziyad. Memorize this sequence.
3
Disagreement Between Sahibayn and Abu Hanifa
Two views: (1) the mufti has free choice (takhayyur), (2) the mufti must prefer the stronger evidence. The second is sound for a mujtahid mufti.
4
Later Times: No Independent Tarjih
In later times, after ijtihad capacity is lost, there is no tarjih by pure evidence. The mufti must follow the detailed rulings (tafsil) established by later scholars.
5
Preferring Among Later Scholars
When later scholars differ, prefer the opinion held by the majority among them, such as al-Tahawi, Abu Hafs al-Kabir, etc.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلَم بَأنَ عَن أبِي حَنِيفَه جَاءَتْ رِوَايَاتٌ عَدَت مُنِيفَه اخْتَارَ مِنْهَا بَعْضَهَا وَالبَاقِي يَخْتَارُ مِنه سَائِرُ الرفاق فَلَمْ يَكُنْ لِغَيْره جَوَابُ كَمَا عَلَيْهِ أَقْسَمَ الأَصْحَابُ وَحَيْثُ لَمْ يُوْجَدْ لَهُ اخْتِيَارُ فَقَولُ يَعْقُوبَ هُوَ المُختَارُ ثُمَّ مُحَمَّدٌ فَقَولُهُ الحَسَنُ ثُمَّ زُفَرُ وَابْنُ زِيَادِ الحَسَنُ وَقِيلَ بِالتَخيير في فتواه إن خالف الإمامَ صَاحِـبــاه وَقِيلَ مَنْ دَلِيلُه أَقْوَى رُجح وَذَا لِمُفتِ ذِي اجْتِهَادِ الأَصَحَ فَالآنَ لا تَرْحِيْحَ بِالدليل فَلَيْسَ إِلا الْقَوْلُ بِالتَفْصِيل مَا لَمْ يَكُن خِلافُهُ المُصَحَحَا ، فتأخُذُ الَّذِي لَهُمْ قَدْ وَضَحَا فَإنّنا نَرَاهُمُوا قَدْ رَجَحُوا مُقَالَ بَعْضٍ صَحْبِهِ وَصَحْحُوا ذَاكَ مَا قَدْ رَجَعُوا لِزُفَرْ مَقَالَهُ فِي سَبْعَةٍ وَعَشَرْ ثُمَّ إِذَا لَمْ تُوجَد الروايه . عَنْ عُلَمَاءَنَا ذَوِى الدِرَايَه وَاخْتَلَفَ الَّذِينَ قَدْ تَأخَرُوا يُرَبِّحُ الَّذِي عَلَيْهِ الأكثر مِثْلُ الطَحَاوِي وَأَبِي حَفْصِ الكَبِير وأبَوَِي جَعْفَرٍ وَاللَيْثِ الشهير
And know that from Abu Hanifa many reports came, exceeding count. He selected some of them, and the rest of the companions select from him. So there was no answer for others, as the companions swore. And where no selection of his is found, then the statement of Ya'qub (Abu Yusuf) is the chosen one. Then Muhammad, his statement is the good one; then Zufar and Ibn Ziyad al-Hasan. And it was said that there is choice (takhayyur) in his fatwa if the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disagreed with the Imam. And it was said that whoever's evidence is stronger is preferred; and this is for a mufti of ijtihad, the soundest view. Now there is no preference by evidence; so there is only the detailed (tafsil) approach. As long as his disagreement is not the corrected (musahhah) one, take what is clear for them. For indeed we see they have preferred the statement of some of his companions and corrected it. That which they have referred to Zufar's statement in seventeen [cases]. Then when no narration is found from our scholars of knowledge, and later ones have differed, prefer the one upon which the majority are. Like al-Tahawi, Abu Hafs al-Kabir, Abu Ja'far, and al-Layth al-Shahir.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage lays down the internal hierarchy of Hanafi opinions. First, from Abu Hanifa multiple opinions are narrated; he chose some, and later companions choose from his remaining. If no explicit choice from Abu Hanifa, the order is: Abu Yusuf (Ya'qub), then Muhammad al-Shaybani, then Zufar, then Hasan ibn Ziyad. When the two companions (sahibayn) differ from Abu Hanifa, two opinions are mentioned: (1) the mufti has choice (takhayyur), and (2) the mufti must prefer the stronger evidence (but this applies only to a fully qualified mujtahid). However, in later times (after ijtihad capacity is lost), no preference by pure evidence; the mufti must follow the detailed rulings (tafsil) established by later scholars. That is, when later scholars have corrected a particular view (musahhah), that must be followed. The passage notes that in seventeen cases they referred to Zufar's opinion. When later scholars differ, preference goes to the majority among them, such as al-Tahawi, Abu Hafs al-Kabir, Abu Ja'far, and al-Layth al-Shahir.

Key Terms
صَاحِبَاه (al-Sāḥibān): The two companions of Abu Hanifa: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani
التَفْصِيل (al-tafṣīl): The detailed approach of following the preferred opinions as determined by later Hanafi scholars
المُصَحَّح (al-muṣaḥḥaḥ): The corrected opinion that is deemed most reliable by later Hanafi authorities

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the poem, when no narration is found from the early Hanafi scholars, and later scholars (like al-Tahawi and Abu Hafs) differ, which opinion should the mufti prefer?
A) The opinion of Abu Hanifa by default
B) The opinion of the majority among the later scholars
C) His own ijtihad
D) The opinion of Zufar

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi mufti in later times must follow the hierarchy of imams and then the corrected opinions of later scholars, preferring the majority view when disagreement persists.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa
beginner
8 min
Hierarchy of Hanafi Authorities for Fatwa
Establishes the order of preference among Hanafi imams

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the correct order of preference among Hanafi authorities for fatwa and apply the rule of tafsil when the Imam's ikhtiyar is absent.

Core Idea
The mufti follows a fixed hierarchy: first Imam Abu Hanifa's choice, then Abu Yusuf, then Muhammad, then Zufar.

This lesson unpacks the classical Hanafi hierarchy for fatwa. When multiple narrations exist from the Imam, his chosen view prevails. Where no choice is recorded, a strict order of his companions applies. Understanding this hierarchy is essential for any mufti to issue rulings consistent with the school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the source of the hierarchy
Recognize that the poem describes narrations from Imam Abu Hanifa and the consensus of his companions on the priority of his ikhtiyar.
The poem's first two verses establish the foundation.
2
Apply the primary rule: Imam's ikhtiyar
When Imam Abu Hanifa has a chosen ruling (ikhtiyar), it takes precedence over all other opinions.
Verse 25: 'there was no answer other than his'.
3
Apply the secondary rule: fixed order
If no ikhtiyar exists, follow the order: Abu Yusuf, then Muhammad, then Zufar and Ibn Ziyad.
Verses 26-27 state the order explicitly.
4
Distinguish alternative views
Verses 28-29 mention two alternative views: takhyir (choice) and tarjih bi al-dalil (weighing evidence). Understand that these are rejected for non-mujtahid muftis.
The later consensus (verse 30) settles on tafsil.
5
Understand the operative rule for today
For contemporary muftis who are not mujtahids, the fixed order (tafsil) is the only valid method; they must not engage in evidence-based tarjih.
Verse 30: 'Now there is no weighting by evidence; there is only the statement by detail'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٢٣- واعلَم بَأنَ عَن أبِي حَنِيفَه جَاءَتْ رِوَايَاتٌ عَدَت مُنِيفَه
٢٤- اخْتَارَ مِنْهَا بَعْضَهَا وَالبَاقِي يَخْتَارُ مِنه سَائِرُ الرِّفَاق
٢٥- فَلَمْ يَكُنْ لِغَيْره جَوَابُ كَمَا عَلَيْهِ أَقْسَمَ الأَصْحَابُ
٢٦- وَحَيْثُ لَمْ يُوْجَدْ لَهُ اخْتِيَارُ فَقَولُ يَعْقُوبَ هُوَ المُختَارُ
٢٧- ثُمَّ مُحَمَّدٌ فَقَولُهُ الحَسَنُ ثُمَّ زُفَرُ وَابْنُ زِيَادِ الحَسَنُ
٢٨- وَقِيلَ بِالتَخيير في فتواه إن خالف الإمامَ صَاحِبَاه
٢٩- وَقِيلَ مَنْ دَلِيلُه أَقْوَى رُجح وَذَا لِمُفتِ ذِي اجْتِهَادِ الأَصَحَ
٣٠- فَالآنَ لا تَرْحِيْحَ بِالدليل فَلَيْسَ إِلا الْقَوْلُ بِالتَفْصِيل
٣١- مَا لَمْ يَكُن خِلافُهُ المُصَحَحَا ، فتأخُذُ الَّذِي لَهُمْ قَدْ وَضَحَا
٣٢- فَإنّنا نَرَاهُمُوا قَدْ رَجَحُوا مُقَالَ بَعْضٍ صَحْبِهِ وَصَحْحُوا
٣٣- ذَاكَ مَا قَدْ رَجَعُوا لِرُفَرْ مَقَالَهُ فِي سَبْعَةٍ وَعَشَرْ
Know that from Abu Hanifa came numerous narrations. He chose some of them, and the rest the other companions choose from. So there was no answer other than his, as the companions agreed. And where his choice is not found, the saying of Ya'qub (Abu Yusuf) is the chosen one. Then Muhammad, his saying is good, then Zufar and Ibn Ziyad are good. And it was said there is choice in his fatwa if the two companions differ from the Imam. And it was said: whose evidence is stronger is preferred, and this is for a mufti of ijtihad, which is more correct. So now there is no weighting by evidence; there is only the statement by detail. As long as his opposition is not the corrected one, so take that which is clear to them. Indeed we see them have preferred the statement of some of his companions and corrected. That is what they returned to from Zufar's statement in seventeen issues.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage delineates the authoritative hierarchy for Hanafi fatwa. It begins by noting that Imam Abu Hanifa had multiple narrations; he selected some, while the remaining were later chosen by his companions (Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, Zufar, etc.). The agreed principle is that when Imam Abu Hanifa has a clear choice (ikhtiyar), it takes precedence. If no choice is recorded, one moves to Abu Yusuf (Ya'qub), then to Muhammad al-Shaybani, then to Zufar and Ibn Ziyad. Verse 28 records that if the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) differ from Imam, there is a view allowing takhyir (choice) for the mufti. Another view holds that a mufti with ijtihad can prefer based on stronger evidence. However, due to later taqlid, the first view (no tarjih by dalil) is abandoned; instead, the later consensus relies on the detailed hierarchy (tafsil) given here. Verses 31-33 clarify that unless a companion's statement has been specifically corrected, we take the clearly transmitted position, and the scholars have actually preferred and corrected some companions' statements, such as Zufar's opinion on seventeen issues.

Key Terms
الاختيار (al-ikhtiyar): The specific ruling chosen by Imam Abu Hanifa among his multiple narrations
التفصيل (al-tafsil): The detailed hierarchy of authorities (Imam, then Abu Yusuf, then Muhammad, etc.) without evidence-based tarjih
الصاحبان (al-sahiban): Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, the two primary students of Imam Abu Hanifa
التخيير (al-takhyir): The view that a mufti has a choice between the opinions of the Sahibayn when they differ from the Imam
الترجيح بالدليل (al-tarjih bi al-dalil): Weighing evidence to prefer one ruling over another

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the hierarchy established in this passage, if Imam Abu Hanifa has no clear choice, which scholar's opinion is taken first?
A) Muhammad al-Shaybani
B) Zufar
C) Abu Yusuf (Ya'qub)
D) Ibn Ziyad

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi fatwa hierarchy follows a strict order: Imam's ikhtiyar, then Abu Yusuf, then Muhammad, then Zufar; later muftis do not weigh evidence.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa
intermediate
6 min
Resolving Conflicts Among Later Scholars
Guidelines for when no early narration exists and later scholars differ

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule of preferring the majority of later Hanafi scholars when no early narration exists, and identify when the mufti must exert ijtihad.

Core Idea
When later Hanafi scholars differ, the mufti follows the majority; only if all are silent does he engage in ijtihad.

This lesson addresses a critical gap: what should a mufti do when the early authorities (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) have no recorded ruling on a matter, and later scholars disagree? The text provides a clear hierarchy: first look to the later scholars (such as al-Tahawi, Abu Hafs al-Kabir), and if they differ, adopt the majority view. Only when no opinion is found from any of them should the mufti resort to ijtihad—but with utmost seriousness and fear of Allah.

Guided Path
1
Check early narrations
First, search for a ruling from the early Hanafi authorities (mutaqaddimun). If found, it is binding.
2
If absent, consult later scholars
Look to the muta'akhkhirun, e.g., al-Tahawi, Abu Hafs al-Kabir, Abu Ja'far, and al-Layth al-Shahir, as mentioned in the text.
Their opinions are secondary to zahir al-riwayah but authoritative in its absence.
3
If they differ, follow the majority
Apply the qawida: 'yurajjahu alladhi 'alayhi al-akthar' — prefer the view held by the majority among them.
This is a practical tarjih, not a formal ijma.
4
If no statement from them, exert ijtihad
Only when no opinion exists from these later scholars may the mufti perform ijtihad, but with serious effort and fear of Allah.
The text warns: 'only a wretched person dares to give rulings' without this caution.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٣٤- ثُمَّ إِذَا لَمْ تُوجَدِ الرِّوَايَةُ عَنْ عُلَمَائِنَا ذَوِى الدِّرَايَه
٣٥- وَاخْتَلَفَ الَّذِينَ قَدْ تَأخَرُوا يُرَجِّحُ الَّذِي عَلَيْهِ الأَكْثَر
٣٦- مِثْلُ الطَّحَاوِي وَأَبِي حَفْصِ الكَبِير وَأبَوَيْ جَعْفَرٍ وَاللَّيْثِ الشَّهِير
٣٧- وَحَيْثُ لَمْ تُوجَدْ لِهَؤُلاءِ مَقَالَةٌ وَاحْتِيجَ لِلإفْتَاء
٣٨- فَلْيَنْظُرِ الْمُفْتِي بِجِدٍّ وَاجْتِهَادٍ وَلْيَخْشَ بَطْشَ رَبِّهِ يَوْمَ الْمَعَادِ
٣٩- فَلَيْسَ يَجْسُرُ عَلَى الأَحْكَامِ سِوَى شَقِيٍّ خَاسِرِ الْمَرَا
Then when the narration is not found from our scholars of knowledge, and the later ones differed, give weight to that which the majority hold. Like al-Tahawi, Abu Hafs al-Kabir, Abu Ja'far (al-Tahawi?), and al-Layth al-Shahir. And when there is no statement from these and fatwa is needed, then let the mufti look with seriousness and exertion, and let him fear the assault of his Lord on the Day of Return. For no one dares to give rulings except a wretched, loser of dignity.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage addresses the situation when no ruling is found from the early Hanafi authorities (Imam Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, etc.). First, one should look to the later scholars (mutakhkhirin) — such as Imam al-Tahawi, Abu Hafs al-Kabir, Abu Ja'far (likely Ahmad al-Tahawi's father or a scholar), and al-Layth al-Shahir. If these later scholars disagree, the majority opinion (al-akthar) is given precedence. If even these later scholars have no recorded statement, and a fatwa is urgently needed, the mufti must exert his own ijtihad (jiddan wa ijtihadan) while fearing Allah's reckoning. The passage warns that only a wretched person dares to give rulings without proper qualification and fear of God.

Key Terms
المتأخرون (al-muta'akhkhirun): Hanafi scholars after the early authorities (Imam, Sahibayn, etc.), typically after the third century
الأكثر (al-akthar): The majority among the later scholars; their consensus or majority view is preferred

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, if no narration is found from early Hanafi scholars and later scholars disagree on a matter, what should the mufti do?
A) Choose the opinion of Abu Hanifa
B) Follow the majority of later scholars
C) Make his own ijtihad immediately
D) Abstain from giving fatwa

Key Takeaway
When early Hanafi narrations are absent, the mufti must follow the majority of later scholars; if they too are silent, he must exercise ijtihad with fear of Allah.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa
intermediate
15 min
Specific Precedence Rules (Dawabit) for Hanafi Fatwa
Versified list of tarjih rules for specific chapters and sources

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply the specific precedence rules (dawabit) for preferring one Hanafi opinion over another in worship, judiciary, inheritance, and source hierarchy.

Core Idea
Rules for choosing among Hanafi opinions differ by chapter: Imam's word in ibadat, Abu Yusuf in qada, Muhammad in dhawi al-arham, and mutun over shuruh.

When multiple opinions exist within the Hanafi school, general maxims alone may not suffice. Later scholars established detailed dawabit (specific rules) to guide the mufti in particular chapters and sources. This lesson walks through those rules as versified by Ibn Abidin, providing a quick-reference framework for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the chapter of fiqh
Determine whether the case falls under ibadat, qada, dhawi al-arham, or general muamalat. This dictates which Imam's opinion is preferred.
The rule for ibadat is absolute unless an exception is recorded.
2
Apply the specific preference rule
For ibadat: prefer Imam Abu Hanifa's opinion. For qada: prefer Abu Yusuf. For dhawi al-arham: prefer Muhammad al-Shaybani. In general, prefer istihsan over qiyas, except in clear cases (mā lā iltibās).
These are not based on evidence strength but on established fatwa practice.
3
Check transmitted reports and prior consensus
If a transmitted report (riwaya) supports a ruling that aligns with diraya (reasoned understanding), do not abandon it. If a mujtahid retracted an opinion, treat it as abrogated. If a saying removes kufr from a Muslim, prefer it even if weak.
These protect the integrity of transmission and avoid unnecessary takfir.
4
Determine source hierarchy
Mutun (foundational texts) are preferred over commentaries (shurūh), and commentaries over old fatwa collections. Within later works, al-Khaniyya and Multaqa al-Abhur have special precedence. For others, rely on what the author chose as the corrected version (e.g., al-Hidaya).
The principle 'what is explicitly stated is most preponderant' applies unless another wording is corrected.
5
Note the assignment of illa
When scholars assign a legal cause (illa) to one opinion and neglect the illa of another, the opinion with the assigned illa is preferred. This is common in al-Hidaya and similar books.
This indicates the author's implicit tarjih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٤٠- وَهَاهُنَا ضَوَابِطُ مُحَرَّرَه عَدَّتْ لَدَى أَهْلِ الْهُدَى مُقَرَّرَه
٤١- فِي كُلِّ أَبْوَابِ الْعِبَادَاتِ رُجِّحَ قَوْلُ الإِمَامِ مُطْلَقًا مَا لَمْ
٤٢- عَنْهُ رِوَايَةٌ بِهَا الْغَيْرُ أَخَذَ مِثْلُ تَيَمُّمٍ لِمَنْ تَمْرًا نَبَذَ
٤٣- وَكُلُّ فَرْعٍ بِالْقَضَا تَعَلَّقَا قَوْلُ أَبِي يُوسُفَ فِيهِ يُنْتَقَى
٤٤- وَفِي مَسَائِلِ ذَوِي الأَرْحَامِ قَدْ أَفْتَوْا بِمَا يَقُولُهُ مُحَمَّدٌ
٤٥- وَرَجَّحُوا اسْتِحْسَانَهُمْ عَلَى الْقِيَاسِ إِلَّا مَسَائِلَ وَمَا فِيهَا الْتِبَاسُ
٤٦- وَظَاهِرُ الْمَرْوِيِّ لَيْسَ يُعْدَلْ عَنْهُ إِلَى خِلَافِهِ إِذْ يُنْقَلْ
٤٧- لا يَنْبَغِي الْعُدُولُ عَنْ دِرَايَه إِذَا أَتَى بِوَفْقِهَا رِوَايَه
٤٨- وَكُلُّ قَوْلٍ جَاءَ يَنْفِي الْكُفْرَ عَنْ مُسْلِمٍ وَلَوْ ضَعِيفًا أُخْرَى
٤٩- وَكُلُّ مَا رَجَعَ عَنْهُ الْمُجْتَهِدُ صَارَ كَمَنْسُوخٍ فَغَيْرُهُ اعْتُمِدْ
٥٠- وَكُلُّ قَوْلٍ فِي الْمُتُونِ أَثْبَتَاهُ فَذَاكَ تَرْجِيحٌ لَهُ ضِمْنًا أَتَى
٥١- فَرُجِّحَتْ عَلَى الشُّرُوحِ وَالشُّرُوحُ عَلَى الْفَتَاوَى الْقِدَمِ مِنْ ذَاتِ رُجُوحٍ
٥٢- مَا لَمْ يَكُنْ سِوَاهُ لَفْظًا صُحِّحَا فَالأَرْجَحُ الَّذِي بِهِ قَدْ صُرِّحَا
٥٣- وَسَابِقُ الأَقْوَالِ فِي الْخَانِيَّةِ وَمُلْتَقَى الأَبْحُرِ ذُو مَزِيَّه
٥٤- وَفِي سِوَاهُمَا اعْتُمِدَ مَا أَخَّرُوا دَلِيلَهُ لِأَنَّهُ الْمُحَرَّر
٥٥- كَمَا هُوَ الْعَادَةُ فِي الْهِدَايَةِ وَنَحْوِهَا لِرَاجِحِ الدِّرَايَه
٥٦- كَذَا إِذَا مَا وَاحِدًا قَدْ عَلَّلُوا لَهُ وَتَعْلِيلُ سِوَاهُ أَهْمَلُوا
And here are fixed rules that have been counted and established among the people of guidance. In all chapters of worship, the saying of the Imam is preferred absolutely unless there is a narration from him by which another takes, like tayammum for one who threw dates. And every branch related to judgment (qada), the saying of Abu Yusuf is selected. And in matters of dhawi al-arham, they gave fatwa with what Muhammad says. And they preferred their istihsan over qiyas except for issues in which there is no confusion. And the apparent of the transmitted is not deviated from to its opposite when transmitted. One should not deviate from dirayah when a riwayah comes in agreement with it. And every saying that comes negating kufr from a Muslim, even if weak, another? And every opinion the mujtahid retracted becomes like abrogated; rely on its other. And every saying established in the matn (texts), that is an implicit preference for it. So the matn are preferred over the commentaries, and the commentaries over the old fatwas that are preferred. As long as another wording is not corrected, the most preponderant is the one explicitly stated. And the precedence of the sayings in al-Khaniyya and Multaqa al-Abhur has a special merit. And in others than these, rely on what they delayed its evidence because it is the corrected version, as is the custom in al-Hidaya and its likes for the preponderant of dirayah. Similarly, when they assigned an illah for one (opinion) and neglected the illah of another.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage provides a series of specific rules (dawabit) for Hanafi fatwa. First, in all chapters of worship (ibadat), the opinion of Imam Abu Hanifa is given absolute preference, unless there is a narration from him that another companion acted upon, like the case of tayammum for one who throws dates (a specific example). For matters related to judicial rulings (qada), the opinion of Abu Yusuf is selected. For issues concerning dhawi al-arham (inheritance by distant relatives), the fatwa is given according to Muhammad al-Shaybani's opinion. Their istihsan is preferred over qiyas, except where the issue is unclear. Then general rules: the apparent meaning of transmitted reports (zahir al-marwi) is not abandoned for a contrary opinion as long as it is transmitted. One should not set aside a well-understood ruling (dirayah) when a transmitted report (riwayah) supports it. Any opinion that removes the attribution of kufr from a Muslim is preferred, even if weak. If a mujtahid retracts an opinion, it is like abrogated; rely on what he later held. An opinion proven in the mutun (foundational texts like al-Mukhtasar) carries implicit preference. Therefore, mutun take precedence over commentaries (shuruh), and commentaries over old fatawa that are themselves preferred, unless another wording is corrected — then the explicitly stated wording is most preponderant. The compilations al-Khaniyya and Multaqa al-Abhur have special priority. For other works, rely on what the authors chose as the corrected version, as is the custom in al-Hidayah and similar books. Finally, when scholars assign a legal cause (illah) to one opinion and ignore the illah of another, the one with the assigned illah is preferred.

Key Terms
الضوابط (al-dawabit): Fixed rules governing preference among conflicting opinions in specific chapters or sources
الظاهر المروي (al-zahir al-marwi): The apparent meaning of a transmitted report, which should not be abandoned for a contrary opinion
المتون (al-mutun): Foundational legal texts like al-Quduri's Mukhtasar, which enjoy precedence over commentaries and fatwa collections
الدراية (al-dirayah): Reasoned understanding of rulings, as opposed to raw transmission (riwayah)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the specific rules in this passage, whose opinion is selected in every branch related to judicial rulings (qada)?
A) Imam Abu Hanifa
B) Muhammad al-Shaybani
C) Abu Yusuf
D) Zufar

Key Takeaway
The mufti must apply specific chapter and source rules: Imam's opinion in ibadat, Abu Yusuf's in qada, Muhammad's in dhawi al-arham, and mutun over shuruh, subject to exceptions and conditions detailed in the rasm al-mufti.

Basic Hierarchy of Preferred Qawls
Rasm rule establishing internal fatwa hierarchy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rasm rule to identify which qawl (Imam, Abu Yusuf, or Muhammad) is preferred in ibadat, qada, and dhawi al-arham.

Core Idea
In worship follow the Imam, in judgments follow Abu Yusuf, in uterine inheritance follow Muhammad — this is the ḍābiṭ muḥarrar.

The Hanafi school does not always rely on the Imam's qawl. A set of verified rules (ḍawābiṭ muḥarrarah) guides the mufti on which qawl to prefer based on the subject matter. This passage from the rasm al-mufti literature lays out the basic hierarchy: ibadat → Imam, qada → Abu Yusuf, dhawi al-arham → Muhammad, and istihsan over qiyas generally.

Guided Path
1
Identify the domains
The passage explicitly names three domains: ibadat (worship), qada (judicial matters), and dhawi al-arham (uterine relatives in inheritance). Also a general rule about istihsan and qiyas.
2
Recall the preferred qawl for each
For ibadat, the Imam's qawl is preferred unconditionally unless a riwaya from him indicates otherwise. For qada, Abu Yusuf's qawl is selected. For dhawi al-arham, Muhammad's qawl is followed.
Memorize the three pairings: ibadat-Imam, qada-Abu Yusuf, dhawi al-arham-Muhammad.
3
Note the exception
The rule states 'unless there is a riwaya from him that someone else took'. The example given is tayammum for one who discarded dates, though the precise application is unclear.
This exception shows the hierarchy is not absolute but based on transmitted practice.
4
Apply the istihsan rule
Istihsan is preferred over qiyas except in cases where there is no ambiguity (no istihsan needed).
This is a separate principle but included in the same passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَهَهُنَا ضَوَابِطُ مُحَرَّرَةٌ عُدَّتْ لَدَى أَهْلِ الْمُهَى مُقَرَّرَةٌ فِي كُلِّ أَبْوَابِ الْعِبَادَاتِ رُجِّحَ قَوْلُ الإِمَامِ مُطْلَقًا مَا لَمْ يَكُنْ عَنْهُ رِوَايَةٌ بِهَا الْغَيْرُ أَخَذْ مِثْلُ تَيَمُّمٍ لِمَنْ تَمْرًا نَبَذْ وَكُلُّ فَرْعٍ بِالْقَضَا تَعَلَّقَا فَقَوْلُ أَبِي يُوسُفَ فِيهِ يُنْتَقَى وَفِي مَسَائِلِ ذَوِي الأَرْحَامِ قَدْ أَفْتَوْا بِمَا يَقُولُهُ مُحَمَّدٌ وَرَجَّحُوا اسْتِحْسَانَهُمْ عَلَى الْقِيَاسِ إِلاَّ مَسَائِلَ مَا فِيهَا الْتِبَاسُ
And here are precise rules that have been counted and established among people of sound understanding: In all chapters of worship, the saying of Imam is preferred absolutely, unless there is a riwaya from him that someone else took, like tayammum for one who discarded dates. And every branch connected to litigation, the saying of Abu Yusuf therein is selected. And in issues of uterine relations, they gave fatwa by what Muhammad says. And they preferred their istihsan over qiyas, except for issues in which there is no ambiguity.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage is a core rasm rule establishing the internal hierarchy of qawls within the Hanafi madhhab. It begins by stating that there are verified rules (dawabit) recognized by the people of intellect. The rule: in all chapters of 'ibadat (worship), the qawl of Imam Abu Hanifa is preferred unconditionally, unless there is a riwaya from him that someone else (like Abu Yusuf or Muhammad) followed. The example given is tayammum for someone who discarded dates (perhaps a specific case). For matters related to judicial rulings (qada), the qawl of Abu Yusuf is selected. For issues of dhawi al-arham (uterine relatives in inheritance), the qawl of Muhammad al-Shaybani is preferred. Furthermore, istihsan (juristic preference) is generally given precedence over qiyas (analogy), except in clear cases where istihsan is not applicable.

Key Terms
ضوابط محررة (ḍawābiṭ muḥarrarah): Revised and firmly established rules (of fiqh and fatwa)
الإمام (al-imām): Abu Hanifa (the founder of the madhhab)
قضاء (qaḍā): Judicial matters, litigation
ذوو الأرحام (dhawū al-arḥām): Uterine relatives (those related through females)
استحسان (istiḥsān): Juristic preference, often overriding qiyas when a clearer outcome is sought

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) Uterine relatives
D) Istihsan

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi mufti must apply different preferred qawls depending on the domain: ibadat -> Imam, qada -> Abu Yusuf, dhawi al-arham -> Muhammad, and istihsan over qiyas generally.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa

The Mufti's Duty: Following Transmitted Reports and Rational Knowledge
Stresses not to depart from transmitted report or knowledge-based understanding

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that a mufti must not depart from the apparent meaning of a transmitted report or from rational knowledge supported by a transmitted report.

Core Idea
When riwaya and diraya agree, the mufti must follow them.

In Hanafi ifta, the mufti must balance transmitted reports (riwaya) from the madhhab's founding authorities with rational understanding (diraya). This passage lays down a fundamental rule: neither can be abandoned arbitrarily. When the two coincide, they are mutually reinforcing and must be adhered to. This lesson unpacks the meaning and application of this rule.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two prohibitions
The passage has two parts: (1) one should not turn away from the apparent meaning of a transmitted text to a contrary opinion; (2) one should not depart from rational knowledge when a transmitted report agrees with it.
These are two separate but related rules.
2
Understand the basis for each prohibition
The first prohibition is because the text is the one transmitted (marwi), implying it is authoritative. The second is because agreement between riwaya and diraya creates strong certainty.
The basis is respect for transmitted authority and confidence from convergence.
3
Apply the rule to a hypothetical scenario
Suppose a mufti finds a clear qawl from Abu Hanifa on a matter, but his own reasoning suggests a different ruling. According to the rule, he must follow the transmitted qawl, not his own reasoning. Similarly, if his reasoning is supported by a transmitted report, he must not abandon it.
This shows the rule in practice.
4
Identify potential exceptions
The rule is general, but later works on rasm al-mufti discuss exceptions when the transmitted text is weak or there is a stronger contrary evidence. This passage does not mention exceptions, so we rely on other principles.
The rule is foundational; exceptions are handled elsewhere.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَظَاهِرُ الْمَرْوِيِّ لَيْسَ يُعْدَلْ عَنْهُ إِلَى خِلَافِهِ إِذْ يُنْقَلْ لَا يَنْبَغِي الْعُدُولُ عَنْ دِرَايَةٍ إِذَا أَتَى بِوَفْقِهَا رِوَايَةٌ
And from the apparent transmitted [text] one should not turn away to its contrary, since it is transmitted. It is not proper to depart from rational knowledge when a transmitted report comes in agreement with it.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage is a rasm rule about adhering to transmitted reports (riwaya) and rational understanding (diraya). The first hemistich states that one should not deviate from the apparent meaning of a transmitted (marwi) text to a contrary opinion, because that text is the one transmitted. The second hemistich adds that it is also not proper to depart from a reasoned understanding (diraya) when a transmitted report (riwaya) agrees with it. This establishes a principle of harmony: when riwaya and diraya coincide, they reinforce each other and must be followed.

Key Terms
ظاهر المروي (zāhir al-marwī): The apparent meaning of a transmitted report or qawl from the madhhab
دراية (dirāyah): Knowledge achieved through reasoning and understanding, as opposed to transmission (riwaya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling about departing from diraya when a riwaya agrees with it?
A) Permissible
B) Not proper
C) Obligatory
D) Recommended

Key Takeaway
The mufti must adhere to transmitted reports and rational knowledge when they agree, ensuring consistency and authority in fatwas.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa
intermediate

Weak Qawl Negating Kufr and Retracted Ijtihad
Rules on weak qawl negating kufr and effect of retraction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that a weak qawl negating kufr is preferred over stronger dissenting opinions, and identify the effect of a mujtahid's retraction on fatwa reliance.

Core Idea
A weak opinion that keeps a Muslim in Islam is relied upon; retracted ijtihad is like abrogated.

This lesson unpacks two essential rasm al-mufti principles that guide a mufti when faced with conflicting transmissions within the Hanafi school. The first principle prioritizes preserving Islamic identity over rigid adherence to stronger proofs: any opinion, even if weak, that removes a charge of kufr from a Muslim must be given preference. The second principle governs the effect of an imam's retraction: once a mujtahid (like Abu Hanifa) abandons a previous ruling, that ruling becomes effectively abrogated and cannot be used for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two distinct rules
Read the couplet and recognize that it contains two separate provisions: one about qawl that negates kufr, and one about retracted ijtihad. Mark the division clearly.
The word 'wa' (and) in 'وَكُلُّ مَا رَجَعَ' signals a new but related rule.
2
Analyze the first rule: acceptance of weak qawl negating kufr
Note the phrase 'وَلَوْ ضَعِيفًا' (even if weak). Understand that this rule is an exception to the normal preference for strong (rajih) opinions. The underlying rationale is the maxim 'al-ghalabah' (giving the benefit of the doubt to avoid takfir).
This rule applies regardless of the strength of the opposing qawl.
3
Analyze the second rule: status of retracted ijtihad
The phrase 'صَارَ كَمَنْسُوخٍ' (becomes like abrogated) indicates that the retracted opinion is no longer valid for fatwa. The mufti must identify which opinion was retracted and which is the surviving, relied-upon one.
Retraction here means the mujtahid explicitly abandons his earlier view; mere silence or later contrary statements may not suffice.
4
Apply the rules to a hypothetical scenario
Consider a case where there is a strong opinion in the madhhab declaring a Muslim's statement as kufr, but a weak opinion says it is not kufr. Apply the first rule: the weak opinion is taken. Then consider if Abu Hanifa initially held a view and later retracted it; the retracted view is no longer operative.
Ensure you distinguish between retraction and mere preference; retraction is a formal abandonment.
5
Identify the practical impact on fatwa
These rules directly affect which opinion a mufti chooses when issuing a fatwa. The first rule forces reliance on a weaker opinion to avoid harm, and the second removes the retracted opinion from consideration.
Both rules are based on the principle of ease and preservation of community.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَكُلُّ قَوْلٍ جَاءَ يَنْفِي الْكُفْرَا عَنْ مُسْلِمٍ وَلَوْ ضَعِيفًا أُخْرَى وَكُلُّ مَا رَجَعَ عَنْهُ الْمُجْتَهِدْ صَارَ كَمَنْسُوخٍ فَغَيْرُهُ اعْتُمِدْ
And every saying that comes negating disbelief from a Muslim, even if weak, is [accepted as] another [opinion]. And every [ruling] from which the mujtahid retracts becomes like abrogated, so another is relied upon.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage gives two separate rasm rules. First: if there is a qawl (statement) that removes the attribution of kufr (disbelief) from a Muslim, it is accepted and acted upon, even if that qawl is considered weak (da'if) in the madhhab. The principle is that it is better to keep a Muslim within the fold of Islam than to declare him a disbeliever. Second: if a mujtahid (here meaning a founding imam like Abu Hanifa) retracts a previous ruling, that ruling becomes like an abrogated (mansukh) text; it is no longer relied upon, and another (presumably the later or surviving) qawl is the one to follow.

Key Terms
كفر (kufr): Disbelief, the opposite of iman; ruling that a person has left Islam
رجع (raja'a): Retraction of a ruling by a mujtahid (such as an imam)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the status of a qawl that negates kufr from a Muslim?
A) It is rejected if weak
B) It is accepted even if weak
C) It is accepted only if strong
D) It is accepted only if from the Imam

Key Takeaway
Two essential rasm rules: (1) Any opinion that saves a Muslim from a charge of kufr is followed, even if weak; (2) A retracted ruling is no longer valid for fatwa.

Hierarchy of Sources: Matn, Sharh, and Fatawa
A poem establishing the mufti's source hierarchy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule of source hierarchy (matn, sharh, old fatawa) and identify the exception based on explicit correction.

Core Idea
Matn texts are the default preference; explicit correction in a later source overrides the hierarchy.

A mufti must know which textual source carries more weight. This poem from the Rasm al-Mufti literature gives a clear ordering: matn (original texts) are implicitly preferred over commentaries, and commentaries over old fatwas. But there is a critical exception: if a qawl in a lower source is explicitly corrected, that corrected version becomes more preferred. This ensures that the mufti relies on the most accurate and verified positions.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the hierarchy
Internalize the sequence: matn > sharh > old fatawa. This is the default order of preference when sourcing a qawl.
Matn = Mukhtasar al-Quduri, etc.
2
Identify the exception
The rule applies 'ma lam yakun siwahu lafẓan ṣuḥḥiḥā' – unless another qawl is explicitly confirmed in wording. Then the explicit correction takes precedence.
Explicit correction overrides the hierarchy.
3
Apply to a scenario
Given a choice between a matn qawl and a sharh qawl that is explicitly corrected, which should you prefer?
The corrected sharh wins.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَكُلُّ قَوْلٍ فِي الْمُتُونِ أَثْبَتُوا فَذَاكَ تَرْجِيحٌ لَهُ ضِمْنًا أَتَى فَرُجِّحَتْ عَلَى الشُّرُوحِ وَالشُّرُوحُ عَلَى الْفَتَاوَى الْقِدَمِ مِنْ ذَاتِ رُجُوحٍ مَا لَمْ يَكُنْ سِوَاهُ لَفْظًا صُحِّحَا فَالْأَرْجَحُ الَّذِي بِهِ قَدْ صُرِّحَا
And every qawl that they have established in the matn (original texts) – that is a tarjih (preference) for it implicitly. So matn are preferred over commentaries, and commentaries over old fatwas that have some weakness. Unless another [qawl] is explicitly confirmed (suhhiha) in wording, then the one that has been made explicit is more preferred.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage provides a hierarchy of textual sources within the Hanafi madhhab. First, qawls recorded in the matn (the original authoritative compilations like Mukhtasar al-Quduri or similar) are given implicit preference (tarjih). Second, matn texts are preferred over sharh (commentaries). Third, sharh are preferred over old fatawa (qadima) that may have weakness (dhāt rujūḥ? Possibly meaning they are from early fatwa collections but not as strong). However, an exception: if a qawl in a lower source (like a commentary) is explicitly corrected (suhhiha) in wording, then that explicit correction takes precedence over the mere presence in matn. The rule ensures that clarity and explicit correction override the general hierarchy.

Key Terms
متون (mutūn): The concise foundational texts of the madhhab, such as Mukhtasar al-Quduri

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the poem, what is preferred over old fatawa?
A) Matn and sharh
B) Only matn
C) Commentaries (shuruh)
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi source hierarchy (matn > sharh > old fatawa) is default, but an explicitly corrected qawl in a lower source takes precedence.

Preference Among Compilations and 'Illa-Based Tarjih
Rasm al-mufti: tarjih of qawls in compilations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the three tarjih principles from this passage to resolve conflicting qawls in Hanafi fatwa works.

Core Idea
Earlier qawl in Khani and Multaqa is superior; elsewhere prefer later supported proof; explicit 'illa gives preference.

When a mufti encounters conflicting qawls in Hanafi compilations, he needs objective criteria to choose. This passage provides three key principles: the temporal priority of the qawl in two specific works, the later attestation of proof in others, and the role of explicit legal cause. These rules help ensure consistent and authoritative fatwa practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the works
First, note the two named compilations: al-Khaniyya and Multaqa al-Abhur. For these, the rule is that the earlier qawl (saabiq al-aqwal) has superiority (maziyyah).
Al-Khaniyya likely refers to Fatawa Qadikhan; Multaqa al-Abhur is by Ibrahim al-Halabi.
2
Apply the rule for other works
For works other than those two, like al-Hidayah, rely on the qawl whose proof was later supported (akhkharu dalilahu). This is because it is the revised version (muharrar) and reflects mature ijtihad.
This is the custom in al-Hidayah and similar texts.
3
Evaluate the 'illa when present
If the authors provide an explicit 'illa (legal cause) for one qawl and omit it for another, prefer the qawl with the explicit 'illa. The presence of ta'lil indicates careful reasoning.
This is an additional indicator of preponderance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَسَابِقُ الْأَقْوَالِ فِي الْخَانِيَّةِ وَمُلْتَقَى الْأَبْحُرِ ذُو مَزِيَّةٍ وَفِي سِوَاهُمَا اعْتُمِدْ مَا أَخَّرُوا دَلِيلَهُ لِأَنَّهُ الْمُحَرَّرْ كَمَا هُوَ الْعَادَةُ فِي الْهِدَايَةِ وَنَحْوِهَا لِرَاجِحِ الدِّرَايَةِ كَذَا إِذَا مَا وَاحِدًا قَدْ عَلَلُوا لَهُ وَتَعْلِيلُ سِوَاهُ أَهْمَلُوا
And the earlier of the qawls in al-Khaniyya and Multaqa al-Abhur has superiority. And in other than them, rely on what they later [supported] its proof, because it is the revised [version], as is the custom in al-Hidayah and its like for the preponderance of knowledge. Similarly, when they gave an 'illa for one and neglected the 'illa of the other.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage adds two more tarjih principles. First: when encountering multiple qawls in the compilations of al-Khaniyya (Fatawa Qadikhan?) and Multaqa al-Abhur (of Ibrahim al-Halabi), the earlier qawl (saabiq al-aqwal) has superiority (maziyyah). Second: in other works (siwahuma), you should rely on the qawl that they (the authors) later supported (akhkharu) with its dalil (proof), because that is the revised version (muharrar). This is the practice in al-Hidayah and similar works, where the later supported qawl is preferred due to the preponderance of knowledge. Third: if they gave an 'illa (legal cause) for one qawl and neglected the 'illa for the other, that indicates preference for the one with the explicit 'illa.

Key Terms
الخانية (al-khāniyyah): A collection of fatwas, likely Fatawa Qadikhan or similar
ملتقى الأبحر (multaqā al-abḥur): A Hanafi fiqh text by Ibrahim al-Halabi
سابق الأقوال (sābiq al-aqwāl): The earlier of the qawls or opinions
أخروا دليله (akhkharū dalīlahu): They later supported/attested to its proof
المحرر (al-muḥarrar): The revised/settled version
العلة (al-'illah): The legal cause or reason for a ruling

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In works other than al-Khaniyya and Multaqa, which qawl should a mufti prefer according to this passage?
A) The earlier qawl
B) The qawl whose proof was later supported
C) The qawl that is longer
D) The qawl of Abu Hanifa only

Key Takeaway
The three tarjih principles—temporal priority in specific works, later attestation of proof elsewhere, and explicit 'illa—equip a mufti with objective criteria to select the authoritative qawl.

Terms of Tarjih in Hanafi Fatwa Literature
Parsing tarjih terms in Hanafi rasm al-mufti

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and rank the key terms used in Hanafi texts to indicate preference between two qawls, and apply the hierarchy when determining the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
When two qawls exist, 'asahh' is the strongest tarjih term, followed by 'sahih' and other indicators.

This passage from Uqud Rasm al-Mufti provides a concise lexicon of the technical terms that Hanafi muftis use to signal which of two conflicting qawls (opinions) is preferred. Mastering these terms is essential for correctly identifying the mu'tamad (relied-upon) position and giving sound fatwas. The passage also notes a subtle disagreement about the relative strength of 'sahih' and 'asahh'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary indicator
The passage first states that when one qawl is explicitly 'suhhiha' (corrected), that is the mu'tamad (relied-upon). This is the clearest sign of tarjih.
2
List the secondary terms
Then it lists other terms that also indicate preference: ashbah (most similar), azhar (most apparent), mukhtar (chosen), awjah (most appropriate), sahih (sound), and asahh (sounder).
3
Rank the terms
The passage explicitly says 'asahh' is more emphatic than 'sahih'. It also states that sahih and asahh are the strongest among all these terms. However, a minority view reverses the order, saying sahih is more emphatic.
The majority (and the text's implied position) holds that asahh > sahih > others.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
Fatwa is given according to the qawl that bears the strongest tarjih term. Therefore, a qawl labeled 'asahh' is preferred over one labeled only 'sahih', which is preferred over those labeled with the other terms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَحَيْثُمَا وَجَدْتَ قَوْلَيْنِ وَقَدْ صُحِّحَ وَاحِدٌ فَذَاكَ الْمُعْتَمَدْ بِأَنَّ فَتْوَى عَلَيْهِ الْأَشْبَهْ وَالْأَظْهَرُ الْمُخْتَارُ ذَا وَالْأَوْجَهُ أَوِ الصَّحِيحُ وَالْأَصَحُّ أَكِدْ مِنْهُ وَقِيلَ عَكْسُهُ الْمُؤَكَّدْ كَذَا بِهِ يُفْتَى عَلَيْهِ الْفَتْوَى وَذَانِ مِنْ جَمِيعِ تِلْكَ أَقْوَى
And wherever you find two qawls and one has been corrected (suhhiha), that is the relied-upon. Similarly, the fatwa is upon the one described as 'ashbah' (most similar), 'azhar' (most apparent), 'mukhtar' (chosen), 'awjah' (most appropriate), or 'sahih' (sound), and 'asahh' (sounder) is more emphatic than it; some said the opposite is emphasized. So by these, fatwa is given, and these two (sahih and asahh?) are the strongest among all those.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage parses the technical terms used in Hanafi texts to indicate which of two qawls is preferred. When a qawl is explicitly 'suhhiha' (corrected), it is the mu'tamad (relied-upon). Similarly, fatwa is given on the one labeled 'ashbah' (most similar to the madhhab), 'azhar' (most apparent), 'mukhtar' (selected), 'awjah' (more appropriate), and the terms 'sahih' and 'asahh'. The term 'asahh' is stronger in preference than 'sahih', though some said the opposite (i.e., 'asahh' is more emphatic). The final sentence indicates that 'sahih' and 'asahh' are the strongest indicators among these terms. The mufti must learn these terms to correctly determine the rajih (preponderant) qawl.

Key Terms
أشبه (ashbah): Most similar (to the madhhab's principles)
أظهر (azhar): Most apparent (based on strongest evidence)
المختار (al-mukhtār): The chosen qawl (often the mufta bihi)
صحيح وأصح (ṣaḥīḥ wa aṣaḥḥ): Sound and sounder; 'asahh' is more emphatic

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which term is considered more emphatic than 'sahih'?
A) Ashbah
B) Azhar
C) Asahh
D) Mukhtar

Key Takeaway
When ranking Hanafi qawls, 'asahh' is the strongest tarjih term, followed by 'sahih', then other indicators like 'ashbah', 'azhar', 'mukhtar', and 'awjah'.

Resolving Conflicting Qawls: Tarjih Factors in Hanafi Fatwa
Didactic poem listing tarjih factors for muftis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply the tarjih factors from Rasm al-Mufti to choose the preferred qawl between two corrected views.

Core Idea
When two qawls are both corrected, the mufti chooses the one with a preponderating factor like clarity, suitability, or majority support.

This passage from the didactic work Rasm al-Mufti provides a systematic list of tarjih (preference) factors. As a mufti, you will often encounter multiple correct opinions; this passage equips you with the criteria to make a sound choice. Note that the default is free choice if both are corrected, unless a specific factor tilts the scale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conflicting qawls
First, confirm that you have two distinct qawls (views) on the same issue, both of which have been explicitly 'corrected' (tasih) in the school.
Tasih means a later scholar declared the qawl to be sahih or rajih.
2
Apply the default rule
If both qawls are corrected, the default is that you may choose either; each is considered mu'tamad (relied upon).
This reflects the principle that both are valid within the school.
3
Check for preponderating factors
Look for any of the listed tarjih factors: one qawl being asahh (more sound), explicitly designated for fatwa, in the matn, from Imam Abu Hanifa, the apparent transmitted (zahir al-marwi), adopted by the majority of great scholars, based on istihsan, increasing benefit for awqaf, more suitable for the time, or clearer in proof.
The factors are not hierarchical; any one can tip the scale.
4
Resolve conflicts or absence of tasih
If the tasih factors conflict (e.g., one qawl is asahh but the other is in the matn), or if no explicit tasih exists, you must look for a general murajjih (preponderating factor) and choose the qawl with the strongest support.
The passage says 'then judge for the one that has a preponderating factor as you know it.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِنْ تَجِدْ تَصْحِيحَ قَوْلَيْنِ وَرَدْ فَاخْتَرْ لِمَا شِئْتَ فَكُلٌّ مُعْتَمَدْ إِلاَّ إِذَا كَانَا صَحِيحًا وَأَصَحَّ أَوْ قِيلَ ذَا يُفْتَى بِهِ فَقَدْ رُجِّحْ أَوْ كَانَ فِي الْمُتُونِ أَوْ قَوْلَ الإِمَامِ أَوْ ظَاهِرَ الْمَرْوِيِّ أَوْ جُلِّ الْعِظَامِ قَالَ بِهِ أَوْ كَانَ الاسْتِحْسَانَا أَوْ زَادَ لِلأَوْقَافِ نَفْعًا بَانَا أَوْ كَانَ ذَا أَوْفَقَ لِلزَّمَانِ أَوْ كَانَ ذَا أَوْضَحَ فِي الْبُرْهَانِ هَذَا إِذَا تَعَارَضَ التَّصْحِيحُ أَوْ لَمْ يَكُنْ أَصْلاً بِهِ تَصْرِيحٌ فَاحْكُمُ الَّذِي لَهُ مُرَجِّحٌ مَا عَلِمْتَهُ فَهَذَا الْأَوْضَحُ
And if you find tasih (correction) of two qawls occurring, then choose whichever you wish, for each is relied-upon. Except if one is sahih and the other asahh, or it has been said 'this is given fatwa with', then it has been given tarjih; or if it is in the mutun, or the qawl of the Imam, or the apparent transmitted, or the majority of great scholars said by it, or it is istihsan, or it increases benefit for awqaf clearly, or it is more suitable for the time, or it is clearer in proof. This is when the tasih conflict, or there is no tasih at all. Then judge for the one that has a preponderating factor as you know it; this is the clearest.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage provides detailed rules for tarjih when two qawls have both been 'corrected' (tasih) or when no correction exists. The default is that if both are corrected, you can choose either (they are both mu'tamad). However, exceptions: if one is sahih and the other asahh, the asahh is preferred; if one is explicitly said to be the fatwa; if one is in the matn (original texts); if it is the qawl of Imam Abu Hanifa; if it is the zahir al-marwi (apparent transmitted); if it is the majority of great scholars; if it is istihsan; if it increases benefit for awqaf; if it is more suitable for the time (zaman); or if it is clearer in proof (burhan). In cases of conflict or no tasih, the mufti must look for a preponderating factor (murajjih) and choose the one with it.

Key Terms
تصحيح (taṣḥīḥ): An explicit statement that a qawl is sahih or rajih
مرجح (murajjiḥ): A factor that gives preponderance to one qawl over another
قول (qawl): A view or opinion within the Hanafi school, usually attributed to a founding Imam
ترجيح (tarjīḥ): The process of preferring one qawl over another using preponderating factors

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT a valid tarjih factor according to this passage?
A) The qawl is in the mutun
B) The qawl is of Imam Abu Hanifa
C) The qawl is shorter in wording
D) The qawl is clearer in proof

Key Takeaway
When two qawls are both corrected, the mufti may choose any, but must apply the given tarjih factors if present to reach a sound fatwa.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa
intermediate
8 min
Rules for the Mufti: Mafhum, 'Urf, Weak Hadith, and Qadi's Restriction
Memorization anchor for rasm al-mufti rules

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four rules from the Rasm al-Mufti regarding the use of mafhum, 'urf, weak hadith, and the qadi's limitation, and apply them to distinguish between permissible and impermissible reliance on weak hadith in fatwa and qada.

Core Idea
Mafhum and 'urf are valid unless contradicting sarih; weak hadith is restricted to necessity or knowledgeable individuals, and qadi cannot rely on it.

In this final passage of the Rasm al-Mufti poem, the author summarizes key principles that govern a mufti's methodology. These rules clarify when implicit meaning (mafhum) can be used, how custom ('urf) carries weight, and the severe restrictions on weak hadith. For a qadi, the prohibition is absolute—any judgment based on weak hadith is void. Hanafi qadis are further bound to follow only the rajih opinion of the madhhab.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the rule of mafhum
Mafhum (implicit meaning) of transmitted reports is to be acted upon, as long as it does not contradict a clear established text (sarih).
2
Recognize the authority of 'urf
Custom ('urf) is considered valid in sharia, and rulings may be based upon it.
3
Identify the general prohibition on weak hadith
Weak hadith cannot be acted upon nor used to answer a questioner, except for a practitioner with necessity or a person of famous knowledge.
The exception applies to both acting and answering.
4
Identify the qadi's additional restriction
A qadi cannot judge by weak hadith; if he does, his judgment is invalid. Moreover, Hanafi qadis are restricted to the rajih of the madhhab.
This is stricter than the mufti's position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَاعْمَلْ بِمَفْهُومِ رِوَايَاتٍ أَتَى مَا لَمْ يُخَالِفْ لِصَرِيحٍ ثَبَتَا وَالْعُرْفُ فِي الشَّرْعِ لَهُ اعْتِبَارٌ لِذَا عَلَيْهِ الْحُكْمُ قَدْ يُدَارُ وَلَا يَجُوزُ بِالضَّعِيفِ الْعَمَلُ وَلَا بِهِ يُجَابُ مَنْ جَاءَ يَسْأَلُ إِلاَّ لِعَامِلٍ لَهُ ضَرُورَةٌ أَوْ مَنْ لَهُ مَعْرِفَةٌ مَشْهُورَةٌ لَكِنَّمَا الْقَاضِي بِهِ لَا يَقْضِي وَإِنْ قَضَى فَحُكْمُهُ لَا يَمْضِي لَا سِيَّمَا قُضَاتُنَا إِذْ قَيَّدُوا بِرَاجِحِ الْمَذْهَبِ حِينَ فَلَدُوا وَتَمَّ مَا نَظَمْتُهُ فِي سِلْكِي وَالْحَمْدُ لِلَّهِ خِتَامُ مِسْكِ
And act upon the mafhum (implicit meaning) of riwayat that come, as long as it does not contradict a clear (sarih) text that is established. And custom ('urf) in sharee'ah has consideration, for that reason ruling may revolve around it. And it is not permissible to act upon a weak [hadith], nor to respond to a questioner with it, except for a practitioner who has necessity, or one who has famous knowledge. However, the qadi does not judge by it, and if he judges, his judgment does not pass. Especially our qadis, since they are restricted to the rajih of the madhhab when they give fatwa. And completed what I have composed in my string, and praise be to Allah, the seal of musk.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This final passage covers several important rules. 1) Mafhum (implicit understanding) of transmitted reports is to be acted upon, unless it contradicts an explicit established text (sarih). 2) 'Urf (custom) is considered valid in sharia, and rulings may be based upon it. 3) Weak hadith (da'if) cannot be used for action or fatwa, except for a practitioner who has a necessity (darurah) or a person of well-known knowledge (but not for general fatwa). 4) A qadi cannot pass judgment based on a weak hadith; if he does, his judgment is invalid. This is especially true for Hanafi qadis who are bound to follow the rajih (preponderant) opinion of the madhhab. The poem ends with a praise of Allah.

Key Terms
مفهوم الروايات (mafhūm al-riwāyāt): The implicit meaning or inference from transmitted reports (as opposed to explicit wording)
عرف (‘urf): Custom or common practice that is not contrary to sharia
ضعيف (ḍa‘īf): A hadith that does not meet the conditions of authenticity

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Can a qadi pass a judgment based on a weak hadith?
A) Yes, in all cases
B) Yes, if no other evidence exists
C) No, and if he does, his judgment is invalid
D) No, but he can use it for fatwa

Key Takeaway
Mafhum and 'urf are valid unless contradicting explicit text; weak hadith is not for general use; qadi's judgment on weak hadith is void; Hanafi qadis follow rajih.

Hierarchy of Relied-Upon Terms in Fatwa
Poem listing the hierarchy of terms for selecting authoritative opinions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the hierarchy of terms used to indicate authority in Hanafi fatwa and identify which term is the strongest.

Core Idea
The term 'asahh' is the strongest indicator of a relied-upon opinion in the Hanafi madhhab.

When a mufti encounters multiple opinions on a single issue, he must know which one to rely upon. The classical Hanafi scholars developed a precise terminology to rank these opinions. This poem from 'Uqud Rasm al-Mufti memorably summarizes that hierarchy, distinguishing terms like 'ashbah', 'azhar', 'mukhtar', 'awjah', 'sahih', and 'asahh' in order of authority. Understanding this hierarchy is foundational for correct fatwa-giving.

Guided Path
1
Recite the couplet
Read the Arabic verse carefully. Notice that it begins with the condition of finding two opinions, one corrected (musahhah). The key terms are listed: al-ashbah, al-azhar, al-mukhtar, al-awjah, al-sahih, and al-asahh.
Memorize the sequence.
2
Identify the function of each term
Each term indicates a level of authoritativeness. Al-ashbah means 'most similar' to madhhab principles; al-azhar is 'most apparent'; al-mukhtar is 'chosen'; al-awjah is 'most appropriate'; al-sahih is 'correct'; al-asahh is 'most correct'. The poet clarifies that asahh is stronger than sahih.
Some ulama hold the opposite order; this is a known ikhtilaf.
3
Apply the hierarchy
When researching a fatwa, look for the highest term available. For example, if a text uses 'asahh' for one opinion and 'sahih' for another, the asahh opinion is given precedence.
If two opinions both have 'asahh', look for additional tarjih indicators.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَحَيْثُمَا وَجَدت قولين وقد صُححَ وَاحِدٌ فَدَاكَ الْمُعْتَمَد
بِنَحْو ذَا الفَتْوَى عَلَيْهِ الأَشبَه وَالأَظْهَرُ الْمُخْتَارُ ذَا وَالأَوْجَهُ
أو الصحيح، والأصح اكد مِنْهُ وَقِيلَ عَكْسُهُ المُوَكَدْ
كَذَا بِه يُفْتَى عَلَيْهِ الفَتْوَى وَذَانِ مِنْ جَمِيعِ تِلْكَ أَقْوى
And wherever you find two opinions and one has been corrected, then that is the relied-upon. By such this the fatwa is given: the 'ashbah', 'azhar', 'mukhtar', 'awjah', or 'sahih'. And 'asahh' is stronger than it, and it is said the opposite is confirmed. Thus by it fatwa is given, and these two are the strongest among all those.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage from the poem 'Uqud Rasm al-Mufti' enumerates the hierarchy of terms used to indicate the strength of a ruling in the Hanafi madhhab. The mufti must know that a 'corrected' (musahhah) opinion takes precedence. Then the terms are listed: الأشبه (most similar), الأظهر (most apparent), المختار (chosen), الأوجه (most appropriate), الصحيح (correct), and الأصح (most correct). It notes that 'asahh' is stronger than 'sahih', though some say the opposite. The couplet ends by stating that these two (sahih and asahh) are the strongest among all those terms.

Key Terms
المعتمد (al-mu'tamad): The corrected opinion among two, the one that is relied upon for fatwa.
الأشبه (al-ashbah): The opinion that is most similar to the principles of the madhhab.
الأظهر (al-azhar): The most apparent opinion in the madhhab.
المختار (al-mukhtar): The chosen opinion among scholars.
الأوجه (al-awjah): The most appropriate opinion.
الصحيح (al-sahih): The correct opinion in the madhhab.
الأصح (al-asahh): The most correct opinion, stronger than sahih.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which term does the poet state is 'stronger' (akad) than 'sahih'?
A) al-ashbah
B) al-azhar
C) al-asahh
D) al-mukhtar

Key Takeaway
The hierarchy of terms aids the mufti in selecting the most authoritative opinion for fatwa, with 'asahh' being the strongest indicator.

Tarjih Between Two Corrected Opinions
Rules for selecting one of two equally corrected opinions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the hierarchy of preponderance factors when two Hanafi opinions are both corrected, and apply them to select the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
When two opinions are equally corrected, choice defaults to free until specific preponderance factors intervene.

A mufti often encounters two equally authenticated rulings in the Hanafi madhhab. This passage from Ibn Abidin's Uqud Rasm al-Mufti lays out the methodology for preponderance (tarjih) when no earlier authority has settled the matter. It begins with a default of free choice, then lists factors that tip the scale in a specific hierarchy.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the scenario
You have two opinions both declared 'sahih' (corrected) by later scholars. Neither has been explicitly chosen. The default is free choice — you may pick either.
This freedom assumes no other evidence of preponderance.
2
Check for explicit tarjih markers
The first exception: if one is labelled 'sahih' and the other 'asahh' (more correct), or if one is stated 'yufta bihi' (this is given fatwa with), that one is preferred. These override free choice.
These markers indicate an earlier authority already made a selection.
3
Apply secondary factors in order
If no explicit marker, consider: appearance in the authoritative mutun (texts), statement of Imam Abu Hanifa, apparent meaning of narrated reports, consensus of great scholars, istihsan, greater benefit to endowments (awqaf), suitability for the time, or clarity of proof. Each factor gives preponderance.
The poem hints at an order: mutun > Imam's saying > apparent narration > majority > istihsan > benefit > suitability > clarity.
4
Determine if factors apply or contradict
The factors apply only when there is no original clear statement (tashrih) about which opinion is preferred. If a factor is present, it tips the balance. If the factors conflict, seek the clearest one.
The phrase 'aw lam yakun aslan bihi tasrih' sets the condition.
5
Select the opinion with the clearest factor
After evaluating, pick the opinion that has the strongest preponderating factor. The poem concludes: 'fa-khudh alladhi lahu murajjih ma 'alimtahu' – take the one you have learned has a preponderator.
This is the operative rule for fatwa selection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِنْ تَجِدُ تَصْحِيْحَ قولين وَرَد فَاخْتَرْ لِمَا شِئْتَ فَكُلِّ مُعْتَمَدْ
إلا إذَا كَانَا صَحِيحًا وأَصَحَ أو قِيلَ ذَا يُفْتَى بِهِ فَقَدْ رُجح
أَوْ كَانَ فِى المُتُونِ أَوْ قَوْلَ الإِمَامِ أَوْ ظَاهِرَ المَرْوِى أو جُلّ العِظَامِ
قالَ بِهِ، أَوْ كَانَ الاِسْتِحْسَانَا أوْ زَادَ لِلأَوْقَافِ نَفْعًا بَانَا
أوْ كَانَ ذَا أوفق للزمَانُ أَوْ كَانَ ذَا أَوْضَحُ فِي البُرْهَانَ
هَذَا إِذَا تَعَارَضَ التَصْحِيحُ أو لَمْ يَكُنْ أَصْلا به تَصْرِيحٌ
فَتَاحُدُ الَّذِي لَهُ مُرَجُح ما عَلِمْتَهُ فَهَذَا الأَوْضَحُ
And if you find correction of two opinions reported, then choose whichever you wish, for each is relied upon. Except if they are 'sahih' and 'asahh', or it is said 'this is given fatwa with', then it is preponderated. Or if it is in the texts (mutun), or the statement of the Imam, or the apparent narrated, or the majority of great scholars said it, or it is istihsan, or it increases benefit for endowments (awqaf) evidently, or it is more suitable for the time, or it is clearer in proof. This is when corrections conflict, or there is no clear statement originally. Then choose the one which has a preponderating factor you have learned, so that is the clearest.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This section presents the rule for when two opinions in the madhhab have both been 'corrected' (i.e., deemed reliable by later scholars). Initially, the mufti has free choice. However, preponderance (tarjih) is determined by several factors in order: if one is 'sahih' and the other 'asahh', the latter is preferred; if one is explicitly stated as 'mufata bih' (the position for fatwa), it is preferred; or if one is found in the authoritative texts (mutun), or is the statement of the founder Imam (Abu Hanifa), or is the apparent meaning of transmitted narrations, or is adopted by the majority of great scholars, or is based on istihsan, or brings greater benefit to awqaf (endowments), or is more suitable for the time, or is clearer in its proof. These factors apply when there is no explicit clarification from the earlier masters. The mufti picks the one with the clearest preponderating factor.

Key Terms
تصحيح (tashih): The act of declaring an opinion to be correct (sahih) in the madhhab.
متن (ج: متون) (matn (pl. mutun)): The short authoritative texts of the madhhab, such as al-Quduri, al-Mukhtar, etc.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference, a method of reasoning in Hanafi usul.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the poem, which of the following is a factor that gives preference to one corrected opinion over another?
A) It is more beneficial to endowments (awqaf)
B) It is the first opinion you learned
C) It is shorter
D) It is from a later scholar

Key Takeaway
The mufti must follow the hierarchy of preponderance factors: explicit markers first, then mutun, Imam, majority, istihsan, benefit, suitability, and clarity.

The Mufti's Sign: Key Definitions and Purpose of the Commentary
Author introduces his commentary and defines key terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define 'rasm al-mufti' and distinguish a mujtahid mufti from a mere transmitter, according to Hanafi usul al-iftaa.

Core Idea
A true mufti is a mujtahid; a non-mujtahid only transmits rulings.

This lesson unpacks the opening remarks of a classical Hanafi commentary on the methodology of fatwa (rasm al-mufti). The author explains why he wrote the commentary and defines what a mufti is and what constitutes his 'sign'. Understanding these foundational definitions is crucial for anyone studying the discipline of usul al-iftaa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's purpose
Read the opening line: 'This is a subtle commentary that I placed upon my poem... to clarify its purposes and to confine its wild and stray ones.' The author wrote to explain and pin down difficult and rare points in his poem.
The poem is about 'rasm al-mufti' (the sign of the mufti).
2
Define 'rasm al-mufti'
From the commentary: 'The sign of the mufti i.e., the marker that guides the mufti on what to give fatwa upon.' This is the methodological framework for issuing fatwas.
Rasm here means a distinguishing mark.
3
Define 'mufti' and distinguish from a transmitter
The passage quotes al-Fath: 'The mufti is the mujtahid. As for the non-mujtahid who memorizes the sayings of the mujtahid, he is not a mufti... his fatwa is not a fatwa but a transmission.'
A true mufti must be a mujtahid; others are narrators.
4
Understand the metaphors 'awabid' and 'shawarid'
'Awabid' (wild animals) and 'shawarid' (stray animals) are metaphors for obscure and rare legal issues that need careful confinement through commentary.
These terms emphasize the difficulty of grasping all legal nuances.

شبه بالأوابد المضامين التي يعسر ضبطها . (مظ)
Reason for writing the book: Al-Khafi(1): This is a subtle commentary(2) that I placed upon my poem which I composed on the subject of the sign of the mufti(3), to clarify its purposes and to confine its wild(4) and stray( ) ones. I ask Him [that He make it sincere for His noble face]. (1) His saying: "Al-Khafi" means the apparent, for it is from the words of opposites; for the subtlety of the Exalted is not hidden from any person, and in every person, or what is meant is that which is hidden from the servant by directing his affair without effort and hardship, and preparing for him the matters of his world and afterlife from where he does not reckon. And Allah is all-powerful. So in al-Tahawi on al-Durr. (2) His saying: "This commentary" the origin of commentary is the spreading of meat and the like, from it is 'expansion of the chest' i.e., its spreading with the light of the unseen, and from it is 'O Lord, expand my chest for me', and it is said its meaning is absolute expansion, and its opposite is constriction; for the Exalted's saying: 'Whomsoever Allah wishes to guide...' and it was interpreted in 'Did We not expand' by the expansion of what is deposited in it of knowledge and wisdom. And commentary here means the explainer or revealer, or making the words a commentary by exaggeration, and the reference to what is in the mind of imagined words indicating meanings. This is the most appropriate of the seven famous aspects. (3) His saying: "The sign of the mufti" i.e., the marker that guides the mufti to what he gives fatwa upon. And the mufti is the one who explains incidents, and in the Shari'ah: he is the answerer in legal matters and secondary cases. He said in al-Fath: The consensus of the usuliyyun has settled that the mufti is the mujtahid. As for the non-mujtahid who memorizes the sayings of the mujtahid, he is not a mufti. And it is obligatory upon him when asked to mention the saying of the mujtahid as the imam by way of narration. Thus it is known that the fatwa of present-day muftis is not a fatwa, but rather the transmission of the mufti's words for the mustafti to follow. (4) His saying: "Its wild ones" said al-Majd: 'Awa'bid is the plural of 'abidah, meaning wild animals, and from it is the poet's saying: And already he went early while birds in their nests, with a stripped horse as fast as wild ones. He likened the meanings that are difficult to grasp to wild animals.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage is the author's introduction and commentary on his own poem. It begins with the reason for writing the book: he composed a subtle commentary on his poem about the 'rasm al-mufti' (the defining mark of the mufti) in order to explain its purposes and to pin down its difficult and rare points. He then asks Allah to make it sincere. The commentary then defines key terms. 'Al-Khafi' is explained as having opposite meanings—it can mean 'apparent' or 'hidden', both applying to Allah's subtlety. 'Sharh' (commentary) is defined linguistically and then contextually as an explainer or revealer. 'Rasm al-mufti' is defined as the marker that guides the mufti in issuing fatwas. The mufti himself is defined as a mujtahid; the non-mujtahid who transmits rulings is not a true mufti but merely a narrator. The terms 'awabid' and 'shawarid' are also defined: 'awabid' (wild animals) are likened to obscure and difficult meanings, while 'shawarid' (stray ones) are rare and scattered issues. This passage sets the stage for the commentary, emphasizing the need for scholarly precision.

Key Terms
رسم المفتى (rasm al-mufti): The sign or marker that guides the mufti on what to give fatwa upon.
مفتى (mufti): In this passage, the mufti is defined as a mujtahid who explains incidents and answers legal questions. The non-mujtahid is not considered a true mufti.
أوابد (awabid): Plural of 'abidah, meaning wild animals. Here, metaphorically refers to difficult and obscure meanings that are hard to grasp.
شوارد (shawarid): Plural of sharidah, meaning stray animals. Here, refers to rare and scattered issues or unusual narrations.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The true mufti is a mujtahid; all others are narrators, a distinction that shapes the entire practice of fatwa.
