Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa
intermediate
8 min
The Three Levels of Masail in the Hanafi Madhhab
Explaining the three levels of legal issues in Hanafi school

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the three tabaqat of masail in the Hanafi school and apply the hierarchy when evaluating a fatwa.

Core Idea
Hanafi masail are ranked by transmission: Zahir al-Riwayah (six books) is highest, then Nawadir, then Fatawa.

Knowing the hierarchy of masail is essential for a mufti. This lesson presents the three levels as explained in Uqud Rasm al-Mufti, enabling you to weigh the authority of different rulings and avoid weak transmissions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three tabaqat
Learn the names and order: Zahir al-Riwayah, Nawadir, and Fatawa wa al-Waqiat.
This classification is unique to the Hanafi school for determining the authority of rulings.
2
Study Zahir al-Riwayah
Understand that it consists of the six books of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, transmitted via reliable chains (mutawatir or mashhur).
These are the highest authority, analogous to Sahihayn in hadith.
3
Study Nawadir
Learn that these are issues transmitted from the founders but not in the six books; found in other works by Muhammad or other early scholars.
Nawadir are like the four Sunan in hadith—lower than Sahihayn but still authoritative.
4
Study Fatawa wa al-Waqiat
Understand that these are later deduced rulings without strong chains; they are only accepted if they conform to usul and have supporting evidence.
Fatawa are the lowest and require careful scrutiny before reliance.
5
Apply the hierarchy
When encountering a ruling, prioritize Zahir al-Riwayah, then Nawadir, then Fatawa. Reject Fatawa if they contradict higher sources or lack evidence.
This principle ensures sound fatwa practice.

Commentary on Uqud Rasm al-Mufti
(1)
The Levels of Issues
That it be the statement of the three, or the statement of some of them. Then these issues (2) which are called the manifest transmission and the principles, are what is found in the books of Muhammad which are: al-Mabsut, al-Ziyadat, al-Jami' al-Saghir, al-Siyar al-Saghir, al-Jami' al-Kabir, and al-Siyar al-Kabir. They are called manifest transmission because they were transmitted from Muhammad through trustworthy narrators, so they are established from him either through mass transmission or widespread transmission.
The second: The issues of the Nawadir, which are issues narrated from the mentioned founders of the school, but not in the mentioned books; rather, either in other books by Muhammad (3) besides these, such as al-Kaysaniyat, al-Haruniyat, al-Jurjaniyat, and al-Raqiyat (4). They are called non-manifest transmission because they were not transmitted from Muhammad through manifest, established, sound narrations like the first books. Or in books not by Muhammad, such as the book al-Mujarrad by al-Hasan ibn Ziyad and others, and among them are the books of al-Amali by Abu Yusuf. "al-Amali" is the plural of imla', which is when a scholar sits with his students around them with ink pots and papers, and the scholar speaks about what Allah opens for him from his heart in knowledge, and the students write it down, then they collect what they wrote, and it becomes a book, so they call it al-Imla' and al-Amali. This was the practice of the predecessors among the jurists, hadith scholars, and Arabic linguists and others in their sciences; it has become extinct due to the passing of knowledge and scholars—and to Allah is the return. The Shafi'i scholars call something similar "Ta'liqah". Or it may be [transmitted] through individual narrations such as the narration of Ibn Sama'ah and Mu'alla ibn Mansur and others on specific issues.
The third: The Fatawa and al-Waqiat (1), which are issues deduced by the mujtahids: during the time of Harun al-Rashid. Al-Jurjaniyat are issues collected by Muhammad (rahimahullah) in Jurjan, and al-Raqiyat are issues that Muhammad ibn al-Hasan derived when he was a judge in Raqqa, or what Muhammad ibn Sama'ah narrated from Imam Muhammad in Raqqa. Whoever wants detail should refer to the index of books at the end of this commentary.
++
(1) His saying: "The third: Fatawa and al-Waqiat". Know that the rank of the six books in principles is like al-Sahihayn in hadith, and the rank of Nawadir in our school is like the four Sunan, and al-Muhit al-Radawi is like Mishkat al-Masabih which collected what is in al-Sahihayn and the four Sunan and others with distinction.
As for the Fatawa: they are of a lower degree than the Nawadir, for what is in them is not entirely from the statements of the founders of the school, and they have no chain of transmission tracing back to their speaker. They were only collected by individuals from the jurists whose state of transmission and soundness of knowledge is not known. Thus, they are not acted upon nor accepted regarding what is not found in the books of principles and Nawadir, except on the condition that they agree with the methodological principles of the school and that evidence supports their correctness. As for the strange narrations that isolated narrators among the authors of later centuries transmit,
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage from the commentary of Uqud Rasm al-Mufti explains the three levels (tabaqat) of masail in the Hanafi madhhab. The author first defines the highest level: Zahir al-Riwayah, which comprises issues found in the six books of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani: al-Mabsut, al-Ziyadat, al-Jami' al-Saghir, al-Siyar al-Saghir, al-Jami' al-Kabir, and al-Siyar al-Kabir. These are called 'manifest transmission' because they were narrated from Muhammad through reliable chains, and are either mutawatir or mashhur. The second level is Nawadir, which includes issues transmitted from the school's founders but not in the six books. They appear in other works by Muhammad (like al-Kaysaniyat, al-Haruniyat, al-Jurjaniyat, al-Raqiyat, and others) or in works by other scholars such as al-Mujarrad by al-Hasan ibn Ziyad or the Amali of Abu Yusuf. The author explains the method of 'imla' (dictation) used to compile such works, a practice of the Salaf. The third level is Fatawa wa al-Waqiat, which are issues deduced by later mujtahids but with weaker authority. The commentator notes that the six books are like the Sahihain in hadith, Nawadir like the four Sunan, and Fatawa are the lowest. Fatawa are not accepted unless they conform to the school's usul and have supporting evidence, and they lack clear chains of transmission. This classification is crucial for a mufti to know the authority weight of rulings.

Key Terms
ظاهر الرواية (Zahir al-Riwayah): The six books of Imam Muhammad that are transmitted through multiple reliable chains and form the primary source of the madhhab.
النوادر (al-Nawadir): Issues transmitted from the founders but not in the six books; found in other works like al-Kaysaniyat, al-Haruniyat, al-Jurjaniyat, al-Raqiyat, and books by al-Hasan ibn Ziyad and Abu Yusuf's Amali.
الفتاوى والواقعات (al-Fatawa wa al-Waqiat): Issues deduced by later mujtahids, not necessarily from the founders, lacking strong chains of transmission.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Fatawa wa al-Waqiat
C) Zahir al-Riwayah
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi madhhab stratifies its masail into three levels based on transmission strength; a mufti must prioritize Zahir al-Riwayah over Nawadir over Fatawa.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa
intermediate

Classification of Hanafi Fatwa Sources: Zahir al-Riwayah, Nawadir, and Fatawa
Classifying the hierarchy of Hanafi fatwa sources

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three layers of Hanafi authoritative rulings and apply the condition that Fatawa are only acceptable if they conform to the madhhab's usul and evidence.

Core Idea
In Hanafi fatwa, not all books are equal: Zahir al-Riwayah like Sahihayn, Nawadir like Sunan, and Fatawa lowest—accepted only conditionally.

This passage from Sharh Uqud Rasm al-Mufti provides a crucial classification for any mufti: the hierarchy of authoritative sources in the Hanafi school. Without understanding this, a mufti might cite a weak transmission as authoritative. This lesson will help you parse the three categories and their conditions for acceptance, ensuring you give fatwa from the strongest evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three layers
Read the passage and list the three categories: Zahir al-Riwayah (the six core books), al-Nawadir (rarer transmissions), and al-Fatawa wa al-Waqi'at (later derivations).
The passage explicitly numbers them: الأولى، الثانية، الثالثة.
2
Note the hadith analogy
The author compares Zahir al-Riwayah to Sahihayn in hadith, Nawadir to the four Sunan, and al-Muhit al-Radawi to Mishkat al-Masabih. Understand this hierarchy of reliability.
This analogy helps you remember the relative authority.
3
Recognize the conditions for Fatawa
The passage states that Fatawa are of a lower degree; they are only accepted if they agree with the madhhab's usul (foundational principles) and are supported by clear evidence.
Beware of the common error that all compiled books are equally authoritative.
4
Distinguish between fatwa and waqi'ah
A fatwa is an answer to a specific question (mustafti). A waqi'ah is a juristic discussion of an event without being asked. Both terms are used loosely, but the passage shows their technical difference.
This distinction affects the procedural validity of the statement.
5
Apply the rule
In practice, a mufti must first check Zahir al-Riwayah, then Nawadir, and only then consider Fatawa if they meet the conditions. Never give fatwa from a Fatawa collection without verification.
This is the takeaway for issuing fatwa.

++
(۱) قوله : "الثالثة : الفتاوى والواقعات اعلم أن مرتبة الكتب الستة في الأصول كالصحيحين في الحديث، ومرتبة النوادر في مذهبنا كالسنن الأربعة، والمحيط الرضوى كمشكاة المصابيح التي جمعت ما في الصحيحين" و "السنن الأربعة " وغير ذلك مع التمييز .
أما الفتاوى : فهي أقل درجة من النوادر ، فإن ما فيها ليس جميعه من أقوال أصحاب المذهب، وليس له إسناد يرفعه إلى قائله، إنما جمعها أشخاص من المتفقهين لم يعرف حالهم فى الرواية وحسن الدراية، فلا يعمل بها ولا يقبل ما فيها مما لم يوجد في كتب الأصول والنوادر إلا بشرط أن يوافق قواعد المذهب الأصولية، ويقوم على صحته الدليل. وأما الروايات الغريبة التي يتفرد بنقلها أحاد المصنفين من أهل القرون المتأخرة،

طبقات المسائل
المتأخرون لما سئلوا عن ذلك، ولم يجدوا فيها رواية عن أهل المذهب المتقدمين . وهم أصحاب أبي يوسف ومحمد وأصحاب أصحابهما ، وهلم جراً. وهم كثيرون موضع معرفتهم كتب الطبقات لأصحابنا وكتب التواريخ .
فمن أصحاب أبي يوسف ومحمد رحمهما الله تعالى مثل عصام ابن يوسف، وابن رستم، ومحمد بن سماعة ، وأبي سليمان الجوزجاني، وأبي حفص البخارى، ومن بعدهم مثل : محمد بن سلمة، ومحمد ابن مقاتل، ونصير بن يحيى، وأبى النصر القاسم بن سلام. وقد يتفق لهم أن يخالفوا أصحاب المذهب لدلائل وأسباب ظهرت لهم .
وأول كتاب جمع في فتواهم فيما بلغنا "كتاب النوازل " للفقيه أبي الليث السمرقندى ، ثم جمع المشايخ بعده كتبا أخر، كمجموع النوازل والواقعات للناطفي، والواقعات للصدر الشهيد .
فلا يعتد بها ولا يعتمد عليها، ودرجتها كدرجة الفهارس والمجامع المجهولة فاحفظ . (مظ)
معنى الفتاوى والواقعات والفرق بينهما : الفتوى مأخوذة من الفتي وهو الشاب القوى، فكأن المفتى يقوى ما أشكل بيانه فيشب ويصير قويا فتيا، وفى الاصطلاح الإخبار بحكم شرعي لا على وجه الإلزام"، والإفتاء : الإبانة، يقال: أفتاه في الأمر، إذا أبانه له . والواقعات جمع ،واقعة وهي الحادثة النازلة. قال بعضهم لا فرق بين الفتاوى والواقعات والنوازل ، وقيل : هذا على التوسع في الاستعمال، وإلا فالإفتاء لا يكون إلا عن سؤال سائل، وأما الواقعة فهي ما يتكلّم المفتى على الحوادث ولو لم يسأل عنها، وعلى هذا يكون بينهما عموما وخصوصاً وجهيًا، لأن المفتى إذا سئل فى حادثة وقعت فأجاب، يصدق عليه اللفظان كلاهما ، وإذا سأله المستفتى عما لم يقع فأجاب الحكمة ظهرت له، يصدق عليه لفظ الفتوى دون الواقعة، وإذا تكلّم على الوقائع من غير استفتاء فهذا واقعة وليس بإفتاء .
انظر: حاشية مفتى الروم العلامة أبى السعود على شرح ملا مسكين : (۳/۱)
(۱) قوله : "وهم أي المجتهدون المتأخرون .
Layers of Rulings: Non-manifest transmission because they were not transmitted from Muhammad through manifest, established, authentic transmissions like the first books. (They are) either in books other than Muhammad's, such as Kitab al-Mujarrad of Hasan ibn Ziyad and others, and among them are the books of Amali of Abu Yusuf. 'Amali' is the plural of 'Imla', which is when a scholar sits with his students around him having inkpots and papers, and the scholar speaks about what Allah has opened up to him from his heart concerning knowledge, and the students write it down, then they collect what they wrote, and it becomes a book, and they call it 'al-Imla' and 'al-Amali'. This was the practice of the predecessors from the jurists, hadith scholars, Arabic scholars, and others in their sciences; it became extinct due to the loss of knowledge and scholars – and to Allah is the return. The Shafi'i scholars call similar works 'Ta'liqah'. Or (they are) individual transmissions like the transmission of Ibn Sami'ah and Mu'alla ibn Mansur and others in specific rulings. The third category: Fatawa and Waqi'at (1), and these are rulings derived by the mujtahidun: During the time of Harun al-Rashid, and al-Jurjaniyat: these are rulings compiled by Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) in Jurjan. And al-Raqiyat: these are the rulings that Muhammad ibn al-Hasan deduced when he was a judge in al-Raqqah, or what Muhammad ibn Sami'ah narrated from Imam Muhammad in al-Raqqah. And whoever wants detail should refer to the index of books at the end of this commentary.
++
(1) His statement: 'The third: Fatawa and Waqi'at. Know that the rank of the six books (of Zahir al-Riwayah) in usul is like that of al-Sahihayn in hadith. The rank of al-Nawadir in our madhhab is like that of the four Sunan. And al-Muhit al-Radawi is like Mishkat al-Masabih which collected what is in al-Sahihayn and the four Sunan and other than that, with distinction.'
As for Fatawa: they are of a lower degree than al-Nawadir, for not all of them are from the statements of the madhhab's founders, and they have no chain of transmission traced back to its speaker. Rather, they were compiled by individuals from the fuqaha who were not known for their skill in transmission and sound knowledge. So they are not acted upon and what is in them is not accepted unless it agrees with the principles of the madhhab's usul and is supported by evidence. As for the strange transmissions (al-riwayat al-gharibah) that some individual authors from later centuries uniquely transmit,
(then) the later scholars, when asked about that, and they found no transmission from the earlier madhhab scholars concerning it (they are...). They are the companions of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad and the companions of their companions, and so forth. They are many. The way to know them is the books of Tabaqat of our companions and books of history.
Among the companions of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on them) are: 'Isam ibn Yusuf, Ibn Rustam, Muhammad ibn Sami'ah, Abu Sulayman al-Juzjani, Abu Hafs al-Bukhari. And after them: Muhammad ibn Salamah, Muhammad ibn Muqatil, Nasir ibn Yahya, Abu al-Nasr al-Qasim ibn Salam. And it may happen that they contradict the founders of the madhhab due to evidence and reasons that became apparent to them.
The first book that compiled their fatwas, as far as we are aware, is 'Kitab al-Nawazil' by Imam Abu al-Layth al-Samarqandi. Then after him the mashayikh compiled other books, such as Majmu' al-Nawazil wa al-Waqi'at by al-Natafi, and al-Waqi'at by al-Sadr al-Shahid.
Thus, they are not taken into consideration nor relied upon, and their degree is like the degree of indexes and unknown collections. So memorize (this). (Mazhar? Muhzir?)
Meaning of Fatawa and Waqi'at and the difference between them: Fatwa is derived from 'fataya' which is the strong youth, as if the mufti strengthens what is ambiguous in its clarification so it becomes clear and strong. In terminology: it is informing of a legal ruling without binding force. Ifta' is clarification; it is said 'aftahu fi al-amr' if he clarified it for him. Waqi'at is the plural of waqi'ah, which is an event that descends. Some said there is no difference between fatawa, waqi'at, and nawazil. It is said: this is a broadening in usage. Otherwise, ifta' only occurs upon the question of a questioner. As for waqi'ah, it is what the mufti speaks about concerning events even if he was not asked about it. Based on this, there is a general and specific mutual relationship between them, because when the mufti is asked about an event that has occurred and he answers, both terms apply to him. If the mustafti asks him about something that has not occurred and he answers – wisdom appears for him – the term fatwa applies but not waqi'ah. If he speaks about occurrences without being asked, this is waqi'ah and not fatwa.
Refer to: Hashiyat Mufti al-Rum al-'Allama Abi al-Su'ud 'ala Sharh Mulla Miskin: (3/1)
(1) His statement: 'wa hum' i.e., the later mujtahidun.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage from Sharh Uqud Rasm al-Mufti presents the classification of legal sources in the Hanafi madhhab for the purpose of issuing fatwas. The author divides rulings (masa'il) into layers based on their transmission strength and authority. The highest layer is Zahir al-Riwayah, the six core books of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, which are analogous to Sahih al-Bukhari and Muslim in hadith. Below that are the Nawadir—transmissions that are not as widely or authentically transmitted, including books like al-Mujarrad of Hasan ibn Ziyad, the Amali of Abu Yusuf, and individual narrations from scholars like Ibn Sami'ah and Mu'alla ibn Mansur. These are compared to the four Sunan in hadith. The third layer is Fatawa and Waqi'at, rulings derived by later mujtahids, often compiled in works like al-Nawazil of Abu al-Layth al-Samarqandi. The author strongly warns that Fatawa are of the lowest degree; they are not acted upon unless they agree with the fundamental principles of the madhhab and are supported by clear evidence. He also distinguishes the terms: 'fatwa' is an answer to a specific question, while 'waqi'ah' is a jurist's discussion of an event whether or not asked. This classification is crucial for a mufti to know the authority of the rulings he cites.

Key Terms
ظاهرة الرواية (Zahir al-Riwayah): The six core books of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani that contain authenticated transmissions from Abu Hanifa.
النوادر (al-Nawadir): Rulings transmitted through less authoritative chains, not part of the six books, like al-Mujarrad and al-Amalī.
الفتاوى والواقعات (al-Fatawa wa al-Waqi'at): Rulings derived by later mujtahids and compiled in collections; of lower authority than Nawadir.
الواقعة (al-Waqi'ah): An event or case that occurs; the mufti may speak about it even without being asked.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which category of rulings is analogized to the four Sunan in hadith?
A) Zahir al-Riwayah
B) Al-Nawadir
C) Al-Fatawa
D) Al-Waqi'at

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi mufti must distinguish three layers of authority: Zahir al-Riwayah (highest), Nawadir (medium), and Fatawa (lowest, only accepted conditionally).

Distinguishing Fatwa from Waqi'a
Terminological distinction between fatwa and waqi'a

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the defining characteristics of fatwa and waqi'a and distinguish between them in a mufti's practice.

Core Idea
A fatwa is a non-binding answer to a specific query; a waqi'a is any incident the mufti discusses, even without a question.

This passage from a classical Hanafi rasm al-mufti text clarifies two fundamental terms: fatwa and waqi'a. Many students and even scholars use them interchangeably, but the author warns that this is a loose usage. Understanding the precise distinction is crucial for proper ifta methodology, as it determines the scope and obligation of the mufti's response.

Guided Path
1
Analyze the etymology of 'fatwa'
The author derives 'fatwa' from 'fati' (strong young man), implying that a fatwa strengthens understanding. Reflect on how this linguistic origin informs the role of the mufti as one who clarifies and empowers.
2
Extract the technical definition of fatwa
In terminology, fatwa is defined as informing of a legal ruling without compulsion (ighrām), specifically in response to a query (su'āl). Identify the key components: legal ruling + non-binding + triggered by a question.
3
Define waqi'a
Waqi'a (pl. waqi'āt) means an actual incident or occurrence. The passage notes that some equate it with fatwa and nawazil, but properly it refers to any event the mufti discusses, regardless of being asked.
4
Identify the overlap and exclusivity
When a mufti answers a query about a real event, both 'fatwa' and 'waqi'a' apply. If the query is about a hypothetical (something not yet occurred), only 'fatwa' applies. If the mufti speaks about an event without being asked, it is waqi'a but not fatwa.
This shows a partial overlap (umum wa khusus wajihtan) – each term has exclusive and shared cases.
5
Apply the distinction to practice
Consider a real situation: a mustafti asks about inheritance after a father's death. The mufti's answer is both a fatwa (due to query) and a waqi'a (real incident). Later, the mufti writes a general article on inheritance without a query; that is only a waqi'a. Recognize the implications for record-keeping and responsibility.
The distinction affects whether the mufti is obligated to answer (fatwa requires a question) or may volunteer (waqi'a).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
معنى الفتاوى والواقعات والفرق بينهما: الفتوى مأخوذة من الفتي وهو الشاب القوى، فكأن المفتى يقوى ما أشكل بيانه فيشب ويصير قويا فتيا، وفى الاصطلاح الإخبار بحكم شرعي لا على وجه الإلزام، والإفتاء : الإبانة، يقال: أفتاه في الأمر، إذا أبانه له. والواقعات جمع واقعة وهي الحادثة النازلة. قال بعضهم لا فرق بين الفتاوى والواقعات والنوازل، وقيل : هذا على التوسع في الاستعمال، وإلا فالإفتاء لا يكون إلا عن سؤال سائل، وأما الواقعة فهي ما يتكلّم المفتى على الحوادث ولو لم يسأل عنها، وعلى هذا يكون بينهما عموما وخصوصاً وجهيًا، لأن المفتى إذا سئل فى حادثة وقعت فأجاب، يصدق عليه اللفظان كلاهما، وإذا سأله المستفتى عما لم يقع فأجاب الحكمة ظهرت له، يصدق عليه لفظ الفتوى دون الواقعة، وإذا تكلّم على الوقائع من غير استفتاء فهذا واقعة وليس بإفتاء.
Meaning of fatwas, waqi'at, and the difference between them: 'Fatwa' is derived from 'fati' which is the strong young man; as if the mufti strengthens what is difficult to explain, so it becomes strong and firm. In terminology, it is informing of a legal ruling without implying compulsion. 'Ifta' means clarification; it is said 'aftahu fi al-amr' when he clarifies it for him. 'Waqi'at' is the plural of 'waqi'a', which is an incident that occurs. Some say there is no difference between fatwas, waqi'at, and nawazil. It is said that this is a broad usage; otherwise, ifta is only in response to a question. As for waqi'a, it is what the mufti says about incidents even if not asked. On this basis, there is a partial overlap: if the mufti is asked about an incident and answers, both terms apply; if the questioner asks about something that hasn't occurred and the mufti answers, fatwa applies but not waqi'a; if the mufti speaks about incidents without being asked, it is waqi'a and not fatwa.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage is a rasm al-mufti definition distinguishing two key terms: fatwa and waqi'a. The author derives 'fatwa' from the Arabic root meaning strength, symbolizing how a fatwa clarifies and strengthens understanding. In technical usage, fatwa is a non-binding legal answer given in response to a specific query. 'Waqi'a' (pl. waqi'at) refers to an actual occurrence or event. The key difference: fatwa always requires a question, while waqi'a does not. There is overlap when a mufti answers about a real event; both terms apply. But if a mufti comments on an event without being asked, it is waqi'a only, not fatwa. The passage criticizes loose usage where both terms and 'nawazil' are used interchangeably. The Hanafi school emphasizes this distinction for precision in issuing fatwas.

Key Terms
الفتوى (al-fatwa): Informing of a legal ruling without implying compulsion, specifically in response to a query.
الواقعة (al-wāqi‘a): A real incident or occurrence that the mufti discusses, whether or not asked.
الإفتاء (al-iftā’): The act of clarifying or explaining; technically, issuing a fatwa.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) A fatwa is about future events; a waqi'a is about past events.
D) A fatwa is from the mufti; a waqi'a is from the mustafti.

Key Takeaway
Fatwa requires a question; waqi'a does not. This distinction is foundational for proper ifta practice.

The Four Strata of Hanafi Issues for the Mufti
Classification of Hanafi legal issues for mufti practice

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any Hanafi legal issue into one of the four strata and apply the correct ruling for fatwa accordingly.

Core Idea
A mufti's ruling depends on the stratum: Zahir al-Riwaya is always binding; shadhdha only if consistent with usul; majority takhrij always; minority takhrij needs verification.

This passage from a work on Rasm al-Mufti presents the essential hierarchy that every Hanafi mufti must internalize. Not all transmitted rulings carry equal weight – some are unconditionally authoritative, others require scrutiny against principles (usul) and analogies (nazair). The classification by Imam Wali Allah al-Dahlawi provides a clear decision tree.

Guided Path
1
Identify the stratum: Zahir al-Riwaya
First, determine if the ruling comes from the six core books of Imam Muhammad (the works of Zahir al-Riwaya). These are accepted unconditionally, even if they contradict apparent principles.
This stratum is always preferred over others.
2
Examine for rare transmissions (shadhdha)
Check if the ruling is a solitary narration (riwaya shadhdha) from Abu Hanifa or his disciples that contradicts the majority. Such rulings are only acceptable if they align with established usul (principles).
If inconsistent with usul, reject it.
3
Distinguish between majority and minority takhrij
For derivations (takhrij) by later scholars (muta'akhkhirin), separate those upon which the majority of Hanafi scholars agreed from those where they differed. Majority-agreed takhrij carries the same weight as Zahir al-Riwaya.
Minority takhrij requires further verification.
4
Apply the decision rule for minority takhrij
When encountering a minority takhrij, the mufti must compare it with the usul and nazair of the earlier masters. If it matches, it can be adopted; otherwise, it must be set aside.
This final step ensures consistency with the school's foundational principles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المتأخرون لما سئلوا عن ذلك، ولم يجدوا فيها رواية عن أهل المذهب المتقدمين . وهم أصحاب أبي يوسف ومحمد وأصحاب أصحابهما ، وهلم جراً. وهم كثيرون موضع معرفتهم كتب الطبقات لأصحابنا وكتب التواريخ . فمن أصحاب أبي يوسف ومحمد رحمهما الله تعالى مثل عصام ابن يوسف، وابن رستم، ومحمد بن سماعة ، وأبي سليمان الجوزجاني، وأبي حفص البخارى، ومن بعدهم مثل : محمد بن سلمة، ومحمد ابن مقاتل، ونصير بن يحيى، وأبى النصر القاسم بن سلام. وقد يتفق لهم أن يخالفوا أصحاب المذهب لدلائل وأسباب ظهرت لهم . وأول كتاب جمع في فتواهم فيما بلغنا "كتاب النوازل " للفقيه أبي الليث السمرقندى ، ثم جمع المشايخ بعده كتبا أخر، كمجموع النوازل والواقعات للناطفي، والواقعات للصدر الشهيد . فلا يعتد بها ولا يعتمد عليها، ودرجتها كدرجة الفهارس والمجامع المجهولة فاحفظ . (مظ) ثم ذكر المتأخرون هذه المسائل مختلطة غير متميزة، كما في "فتاوى قاضيخان" و "الخلاصة " وغيرهما . وميز بعضهم كما في كتاب المحيط لرضى الدين السرخسي، فإنه ذكر أولا مسائل الأصول، ثم النوادر ، ثم الفتاوى، ونعم ما فعل. مطلب في نسخ " المبسوط وشروحه : واعلم أن مسخ المبسوط المروى عن محمد متعددة، وأظهرها مبسوط أبي سليمان الجوزجاني، وشرح المبسوط جماعة من المتأخرين، مثل شيخ الإسلام. تنبيه : هناك تقسيمان آخران لطبقات المسائل ذكر أحدهما الإمام الشيخ ولى الله الدهلوى في كتابه "عقد الجيد في أحكام الاجتهاد والتقليد والآخر ذكره الإمام العلامة عبد الحي اللكنوى رحمه الله في النافع الكبير لمن طالع الجامع الصغير " ، وذكرناه عند قول المصنف : اعلم أن مسائل أصحابنا الحنفية على ثلاث طبقات ونذكر الأول هنا فنقول : حاصل ما ذكره الإمام الدهلوى أن طبقات المسائل على أربعة أقسام : القسم الأول : ما تقرر في ظاهر الرواية، وحكمه : أنهم يقبلونه في كل حال وافق الأصول أو خالف . القسم الثاني : هو رواية شاذة عن أبي حنيفة وصاحبيه، وحكمه : أنهم لا يقبلونه إلا إذا وافق الأصول. القسم الثالث : هو تخريج المتأخرين الذي اتفق عليه جمهور الأصحاب، وحكمه: أنهم يفتون به على كل حال . القسم الرابع : هو تخريج المتأخرين الذى لم يتفق عليه جمهور الأصحاب، وحكمه أن يعرضه المفتى على الأصول والنظائر من كلام السلف، فإن وجده موافقا للأصول والنظائر أخذ به وإلا تركه .
The later scholars (muta'akhkhirin) when asked about that and found no transmission from the earlier masters of the madhhab. They are the companions of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad and the companions of their companions, and so on. They are many; knowledge of them is found in the books of classes (tabaqat) of our scholars and history books. Among the companions of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on them) are: 'Isam ibn Yusuf, Ibn Rustam, Muhammad ibn Sama'a, Abu Sulayman al-Juzjani, Abu Hafs al-Bukhari; and after them: Muhammad ibn Salama, Muhammad ibn Muqatil, Nasir ibn Yahya, Abu al-Nasr al-Qasim ibn Salam. It may happen that they contradict the people of the madhhab due to proofs and reasons that became apparent to them. The first book compiled on their fatwas as far as we know is 'Kitab al-Nawazil' by al-Faqih Abu al-Layth al-Samarqandi. Then later shaykhs compiled other books like Majmu' al-Nawazil wa al-Waqi'at by al-Natafi, and al-Waqi'at by al-Sadr al-Shahid. So they are not considered authoritative nor relied upon; their rank is like that of unknown indexes and collections – so memorize [that] (maz). Then the later scholars mentioned these issues mixed without distinction, as in Fatawa Qadikhan and al-Khulasa and others. Some distinguished them like in Kitab al-Muhit by Radi al-Din al-Sarakhsi: he first mentioned the issues of usul, then nawadir, then fatawa – excellent what he did. Section on manuscripts of al-Mabsut and its commentaries: Know that the manuscripts of al-Mabsut transmitted from Muhammad are multiple, the most apparent is the Mabsut of Abu Sulayman al-Juzjani. A group of later scholars wrote commentaries on it, such as Shaykh al-Islam. Note: There are two other divisions of the strata of issues: one mentioned by Imam Shaykh Wali Allah al-Dahlawi in his book 'Aqd al-Jid fi Ahkam al-Ijtihad wa al-Taqlid', and the other mentioned by Imam al-Allama Abd al-Hayy al-Lucknawi in al-Nafi' al-Kabir. We mentioned it at the author's statement: 'Know that the issues of our Hanafi scholars are of three strata.' We mention the first here: The gist of what Imam al-Dahlawi mentioned is that the strata of issues are four divisions: First division: What is established in Zahir al-Riwaya – its ruling: they accept it in every case, whether it agrees with principles or not. Second division: A rare transmission (riwaya shadhdha) from Abu Hanifa and his two companions – its ruling: they only accept it if it agrees with principles. Third division: The derivation (takhrij) of the later scholars upon which the majority of the companions agreed – its ruling: they give fatwa by it in every case. Fourth division: The derivation of the later scholars upon which the majority did not agree – its ruling: the mufti should compare it with the principles and analogies from the statements of the salaf; if he finds it agreeing, he takes it; otherwise he leaves it.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage is a rasm al-mufti discussion about the strata (tabaqat) of Hanafi legal issues and the standing of various books. It first identifies the muta'akhkhirin (later scholars) – those beyond the direct students of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad. They are defined as those who found no transmission from the earlier masters, so they themselves exercised ijtihad. The author lists names like 'Isam ibn Yusuf and Abu Sulayman al-Juzjani. These later scholars might contradict earlier positions based on new evidence. Their fatwas were compiled in books like Kitab al-Nawazil by Abu al-Layth al-Samarqandi. However, these books are considered unreliable, comparable to unknown indices – not to be relied upon (la yu'taddu biha). Then the passage notes that later compilations like Fatawa Qadikhan mixed different strata without distinction, unlike Kitab al-Muhit by Radi al-Din al-Sarakhsi which clearly separated usul, nawadir, and fatawa. Next, the discussion turns to the manuscripts of al-Mabsut of Imam Muhammad; the most authoritative is by Abu Sulayman al-Juzjani. Finally, a long footnote presents al-Dahlawi's four-fold division: (1) Zahir al-Riwaya – always accepted, (2) rare transmissions (shadhdha) – accepted only if consistent with usul, (3) majority consensus of later takhrij – accepted always, (4) minority later takhrij – accepted only after checking against usul and nazair. This classification governs a mufti's practice when faced with conflicting material.

Key Terms
ظاهر الرواية (Ẓāhir al-Riwāya): Rulings transmitted through the six core books of Imam Muhammad (al-Mabsut, al-Jami' al-Saghir, etc.) considered the most authentic within the Hanafi school.
رواية شاذة (Riwāya Shādhdha): A rare transmission from Abu Hanifa or his companions that conflicts with the majority of transmissions.
تخريج المتأخرين (Takhrij al-Muta'akhkhirīn): Legal derivations by later Hanafi scholars based on principles, not explicit texts from the founding Imams.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Dahlawi's four-part classification, what should a mufti do with a non-majority takhrij from later scholars?
A) Accept it unconditionally.
B) Reject it outright.
C) Compare it with usul and nazair; accept only if consistent.
D) Refer it to a higher authority.

Key Takeaway
A Hanafi mufti must always determine the stratum of a ruling before applying it; Zahir al-Riwaya and majority takhrij are unconditionally binding, while rare transmissions and minority takhrij require verification against principles.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa
intermediate
6 min
The Four-Tier Classification of Hanafi Masail (Rasm al-Mufti)
Rasm al-Mufti rule: classification of masail into four tiers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any Hanafi mas'alah into one of the four tiers and state the rule for accepting it in fatwa according to the Hanafi rasm al-mufti.

Core Idea
Hanafi rulings have four tiers: accept unconditionally, accept if agrees with usul, accept if majority agreed, or scrutinize.

A mufti must know the authority of the ruling he is using. Not all Hanafi statements carry the same weight. Imam Wali Allah al-Dihlawi provides a clear four-tier framework that later scholars adopted. This lesson unpacks that framework so you can prioritize your fatwa sources correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the tier of the ruling
When you encounter a Hanafi ruling, first determine whether it comes from Zahir al-Riwayah, a rare narration (shadhdh), a takhrij with majority agreement, or a takhrij without majority agreement.
Zahir al-Riwayah rulings are the six books of Imam Muhammad.
2
Apply the acceptance rule
For Zahir al-Riwayah: accept unconditionally. For rare narrations: accept only if it agrees with known usul. For majority-agreed takhrij: accept unconditionally. For non-majority takhrij: the mufti must check it against usul and nazair.
The fourth tier requires active research by the mufti.
3
If checking the fourth tier, compare to usul and nazair
Look at the established principles (usul) and parallel rulings (nazair) from the early imams. If the takhrij agrees, adopt it; otherwise leave it.
This preserves consistency and avoids arbitrary fatwa.

Then the later scholars mentioned these rulings intermixed without distinction, as in 'Fatawa Qadikhan' and 'al-Khulasa' and others. And some distinguished them, as in the book 'al-Muhit' by Radi al-Din al-Sarakhsi, for he mentioned first the rulings of al-Usul, then al-Nawadir, then al-Fatawa. And excellent is what he did. 

Section on the copies of 'al-Mabsut' and its commentaries.
Know that the copies of al-Mabsut narrated from Muhammad are multiple, and the most apparent is the Mabsut of Abu Sulayman al-Juzjani. A group of later scholars commented on al-Mabsut, such as Shaykh al-Islam Bakr known as Khahar Zada (called al-Mabsut al-Kabir), and Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani and others. Their Mabsuts are in fact commentaries; they mentioned them intermixed with the Mabsut of Muhammad, just as the commentators of 'al-Jami' al-Saghir' did, such as Fakhr al-Islam and Qadikhan and others. So it is said: 'Qadikhan mentioned it in al-Jami' al-Saghir', but the intent is his commentary. Similarly in other works. End, summarized from the commentary of al-Bayri on al-Ashbah, and the commentary of Shaykh Isma'il al-Nabulsi on Sharh al-Durar.

(1) His saying: 'and others' like al-Fatawa al-Sirajiyya and al-Muhit al-Burhani. (from margin)
(2) His saying: 'And excellent is what he did.' Appendix: There are two other divisions regarding the categories of rulings. One was mentioned by Imam al-Shaykh Wali Allah al-Dihlawi in his book 'Aqd al-Jayyid fi Ahkam al-Ijtihad wa al-Taqlid', and the other was mentioned by Imam al-Allama Abd al-Hayy al-Laknawi in 'al-Nafi' al-Kabir liman Tali'a al-Jami' al-Saghir'. We mentioned it at the author's saying: 'Know that the rulings of our Hanafi companions are of three categories.' We mention the first here: The summary of what Imam al-Dihlawi mentioned is that the categories of rulings are four divisions: The first division: what is established in Zahir al-Riwayah. Its ruling: they accept it in every case, whether it agrees with the principles or opposes them. The second division: a rare (shadhdh) narration from Abu Hanifa and his two companions. Its ruling: they do not accept it unless it agrees with the principles. The third division: the derivation (takhrij) of later scholars upon which the majority of companions agreed. Its ruling: they give fatwa by it in every case. The fourth division: the derivation of later scholars upon which the majority of companions did not agree. Its ruling: the mufti should compare it to the principles and parallels (nazair) from the statements of the predecessors. If he finds it in agreement with the principles and parallels, he adopts it; otherwise he leaves it.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage is a methodological discussion about the classification of Hanafi rulings (masail) according to their source and authority. The author first notes that later scholars (mutakhkhirin) such as those who compiled Fatawa Qadikhan and al-Khulasa mixed together rulings from different categories: Usul (Zahir al-Riwayah), Nawadir (rare narrations), and Fatawa (later fatwas). He praises Radi al-Din al-Sarakhsi's al-Muhit for distinguishing these categories clearly. Then the author discusses the multiple copies (nuskh) of Imam Muhammad's al-Mabsut, noting that the most common is that of Abu Sulayman al-Juzjani, and that later commentaries often merged their own content with the original text, similar to the practice of commentators of al-Jami' al-Saghir. The marginal annotation provides a crucial framework: Imam Wali Allah al-Dihlawi's four-tier classification of Hanafi rulings. The first tier is Zahir al-Riwayah (the core transmitted rulings from the six books of Imam Muhammad), which are accepted unconditionally. The second tier is shadhdh (isolated) narrations from Abu Hanifa or his disciples, accepted only if they agree with Usul principles. The third tier is the takhrij of later scholars that gained consensus of the majority; these are followed unconditionally. The fourth tier is takhrij without majority consensus; the mufti must examine it against the Usul and nazair (parallel cases) from earlier scholars. This classification is essential for a mufti to prioritize rulings correctly.

Key Terms
ظاهر الرواية (Zahir al-Riwayah): The rulings transmitted in the six books of Imam Muhammad (al-Mabsut, al-Jami' al-Saghir, etc.) that are the primary source for fatwa.
النوادر (al-Nawadir): Rare narrations from the founding imams that are not part of Zahir al-Riwayah.
تخريج المتأخرين (Takhrij al-Muta'akhkhirin): Derivations and rulings made by later Hanafi scholars based on the principles of the imams.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the four-tier classification by Imam Wali Allah al-Dihlawi, what is the ruling on a takhrij of a later scholar that was not agreed upon by the majority of companions?
A) It is unconditionally accepted.
B) It is rejected outright.
C) The mufti must compare it to the principles and parallels from the predecessors.
D) It is considered equal to Zahir al-Riwayah.

Key Takeaway
A mufti must always identify the tier of a ruling before giving fatwa, with Zahir al-Riwayah as the highest authority.
